Abstract
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate the possibility for improving the operation of
the heating system in a multi-storey building.
The focus is on a strategy to lower the operating temperatures of heating systems and
stimulate the user’s interaction with the heating system without decreasing the thermal
comfort.
The method of pursuing the desired outcome is utilising existing sensor data from the
building, such as the heat cost allocators and temperature sensors. A software is developed
to translate the data to an understandable form which can substantiate the state of per-
formance of the building down to radiator level. In translating the data, existing radiator
theories are used.
The results show that such a software could describe the performance of a multi-storey
building at a coarse resolution. The software show, however, that few residents are stim-
ulated and reacting to the reduction of the supply temperature. This fact is the basis for
some complaints from the users.
The conclusion is that the software can be serviceable for a building technician in detecting
improper balances and to successfully lowering the operating temperatures.
In further investigation such a software should be used at the same time as lowering the
operative temperatures in order to verify the software’s usability.

tem.
Fokus er lagt p˚a en strategi for at sænke varmesystemets operative temperature uden at g˚a
p˚a kompromis med den termiske komfort.
Metoden til at opn˚a det ønskede resultat er at anvende bygningens eksisterende sensor

Chapter 1. Introduction
1 Introduction
1.1
Background
These days the operation of the building sector stand for approx. 17.5% of the CO2eq.
emission, where 10.9% are for the residential buildings[Lab]. The largest contributors of
CO2eq. emission in Denmark is electricity and heating. The source of information[Lab]
uses data from climate watch, which World Resources Institute manages. Other sources
estimate the building sector to have a part of the emission as high as 38%.[BC](with indirect
emissions).
In order to obtain the climate goal of the Danish government, it is essential to examine
this sector to establish any potential reductions of CO2eq. emissions. If the ﬁeld of study
is narrowed further to consider the operation of the heating system alone and the optimal
approach for doing so, there are tons of reports venturing this.
However, buildings are still in many cases operating at a stage of suboptimal control.This
could indicate and be conceived as a poor dissemination of the right information accumu-
lated by theoretical and experimental dissertations, or it could be something as banal as
missing information on the building’s real-time operation with a proper resolution of details,
which makes it possible for the operator to understand what is going on in the building.
This thesis will investigate the ﬁeld of operation strategy in buildings operated by radiator
heating and try to develop a tool that an operator will be able to use in the daily operation
of the building. The thesis will try to translate the results from previous reports and make
them available and easily usable in the form of a dashboard. By enlightening the status of a
building, the hope is that the operator will be able to pursue the optimal operation. Thus
minimising the supply and return temperature of the system and ultimately minimising
the required supply temperature for the operation of the building. By doing so, it makes
it possible to ﬁnd renewable substitutes for energy production.
Page 1 of 128 pages
==PAGE:16==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 2. State of the art
In Denmark, a large share of the heating operation of buildings is based on district heating.
Studies show that it is possible to lower the supply temperature to a level of 40-60◦C.[Sch18]
If it is possible to lower the required supply temperature for the operation, it is possible to
upgrade the energy production for the district heating to a heat-pump based system and
solar collectors. By converting the system to a low-temperature district heating system
(LTDH) the heat loss in the piping can be reduced from approx. 41% to approx. 14%.
[Sch18] and an overall network energy loss reduction of up to 25%. The action will also
increase the production eﬃciency of the heat. If the performance of the buildings, includ-
ing how to increase the performance, is presented for the responsible and the payers, the
economic beneﬁts of improving the building operation may be stimulated by the building
users to increase the performance by themselves and thus making it possible to lower the
return temperature.
Another way to stimulate the users to balance the heating system is to lower the supply
temperature to the radiators. The users should then open all heating providers in order
to get the required heating delivered.
One major issue with this approach is that the
operator does not have information on a detailed resolution to handle complaints. If any
user complaints, the building operator does not have a straightforward approach to detect
any causes and will thus, in most cases, increase the supply temperature of the system
again.
There are therefore two valid cases where the progress of optimising the system would
beneﬁt from an easy information source. As mentioned an approach to resolve or counter
this need will be the main focus of this thesis.
1.2
Intention of work
This thesis aims to investigate a strategy to lower the operating temperatures of heating
systems and stimulate the user’s interaction with the heating system.

==PAGE:17==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.

Chapter 2. State of the art
2 State of the art
In the ph.D thesis ”SMART ENERGY GRIDS Utilisation of Space Heating Flexibility”
written by Johan Kensby, the possibility of lowering the overall cost of the operation of
buildings by utilising a responsive smart grid and the thermal inertia in the buildings was
investigated. The cases investigated are buildings connected to the district heating system
of Gothenburg in Sweden. There was performed a pilot test on 19 properties, which contain
3-8 storey buildings. The controlling system was modiﬁed to try to utilise the thermal
energy storage of the building. The controlling units were not optimised. 30 properties
were acting as references in the same period.
The ﬁndings of the paper where that in a building of concrete core, it is possible to store
around 0.1 kWh/m2
heatedarea without experience a negative impact on the indoor thermal
comfort. The ﬁndings were that by utilising the buildings’ thermal inertia, it was possible
to reduce the operational costs by approximately 5-10%. The operation cost of reducing
the peak loads on the district heating system by hot water tanks was estimated to be twice
the cost than by utilising the thermal inertia of the building.
The utilisation of the thermal inertia also reduced the peak loads shows potential of reducing
the use of fossil fuels by the heat only boiler (HOB), which provide thermal energy at peak
loads, by up to 25%.
The tests showed that the buildings, which were in the test, all
went from consuming heat in high price periods to consuming in low price periods, just by
utilising a smart controller. [Tr¨u17].
In Maria Jangsten’s master thesis ”Survey of Radiator Temperatures in Buildings Supplied
by District Heating” she also investigates the district heating system of Gothenburg in
Sweden. The main objective was to investigate what the actual temperatures in radiator
systems in existing buildings are through a survey. In the thesis, she looks into historical
data on 109 substations based in diﬀerent dwellings, all owned by Bostads AB Poseidon.
Maria concludes that a way to introduce Low Temperature District Heating (LTDH) could
Page 3 of 128 pages
==PAGE:18==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 2. State of the art
be to implement it in city districts where most buildings are of a newer age. She estimates
that it could be possible to implement LTDH in older buildings if the temperature demand
was reduced which for example can be obtained by lowering the supply temperature. She
estimates, based on the observations on the historical data, that it could be possible to
do with installations present in the building. However, in some other cases, the need of
increasing the heat transfer surface area, the radiator sizes would be present.
One of the survey ﬁndings over the historical data was that the temperature diﬀerence
between supply and return was increasing when the supply temperature was increased.
The ﬁndings indicated that the radiator size and the hydronic balance was some of the
most important parameters for the temperature levels in the circuit.
She also concludes that the temperature set for 4’th generation district heating is not the
most cost-eﬃcient and are maybe set too low. However, this conclusion is based on the
assumption that the temperature demand for the existing buildings is not lowered. The
results showed that the average supply and return temperatures for the design outdoor
temperature of -16°C are 64 and 42°C, respectively.[JAN09]
Hatef Hajian, Kaiser Ahmed, and Jarek Kurnitski describe in an article titled ”Estimation
of energy saving potential and indoor thermal comfort by the central control of the heating
curve in old apartment buildings”, how they simulated the potential energy savings by low-
ering the heating control temperatures. They tried lowering the heating curve from 70/40
to 65/33 and 55/25. They found that the energy savings from dropping the temperatures
did not have a substantial reduction in energy-saving; however it had a signiﬁcant impact
on the thermal comfort, which was improperly aﬀected.
It is worth mentioning that they do not specify any balancing of the system, which is one
of the issues this thesis tries to investigate. They conclude on their ﬁndings that the results
indicate a low to non-energy saving potential by compromising the thermal comfort in the
rooms, even with a change so signiﬁcant as 5◦C under temperature.[AK21].
Page 4 of 128 pages
==PAGE:19==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 2. State of the art
In an article by Ala Hasan, Jarek Kurnitski and Kai Jokiranta, the temperature setting
of the supply and return temperature are investigated. They start by describing the con-
ventional supply and return temperature to be 70/40 for an outdoor temperature of -26◦C.
The theory in the article is that it is possible to lower the temperature set in Finland in a
system containing water-based radiators in the rooms and water-based ﬂoor heating in the
bathroom. They investigate the hypothesis by dynamic simulations via the software IDA
ICE. The system is simulated in apartments in a multi-storey building. The paper indicates
that the design procedure where the radiator width is set to the width of a window makes
the radiators oversized. The oversizing is shown in the simulations to be suﬃcient in size
to deliver the heating at a lower supply/return temperature set. The design procedure is
to some extent the same in Denmark, and the result can therefore be considered applicable
in Denmark.
Furthermore The article refer to an article from Myhren and Holmberg called ”Energy sav-
ings and thermal comfort with ventilation radiators”, where the authors conclude that a low
temperature set on radiators should give a better indoor climate, as the low temperatures
should reduce the convection created by temperature diﬀerence from the radiator to the
ambient air temperature,[Hol07]. Ala, Jarek and Kai investigate the vertical temperature
diﬀerence in a mockup experiment with the low supply/return temperatures. There are no
signiﬁcant temperature vertical temperature diﬀerence, which indicate that a lower tem-
perature set on the heating system does not decrease the indoor thermal comfort. [Ala09]
In the thesis ”HYDRONIC HEATING SYSTEMS The Eﬀect Of Design On System Sen-
sitivity” by Anders Tr¨uschel, the balancing of hydronic heating systems and the eﬀect of
the designing phase of the hydronic system on the overall sensitivity to deviations, in terms
of the eﬀect on performance and return water temperature. His analysis was concentrated
around the diﬀerence between a high-ﬂow and a low-ﬂow heating system. Two diﬀerent
simulation tools were used in the work, a commercial program and a custom-created pro-
Page 5 of 128 pages
==PAGE:20==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 2. State of the art
gram, respectively. The hydronic system was simulated in scenarios with high-ﬂow/low-ﬂow
systems with and without riser and branch valves. A physical system was also made and
analysed in the same scenarios. However, from the measurements, it was not possible to
draw any independent conclusion.
One of the ﬁndings of the paper was that thermostatic radiator valves are most eﬀective
in low-ﬂow systems. Another ﬁnding was that low-ﬂow systems produce the lowest return
temperatures. However, incorrect or changed radiator valve settings can result in substan-
tially increased return temperature and diﬀerences in room temperatures in such systems.
It was found that branch, riser and main valves all reduce the interaction between radia-
tors if one or more of the radiator valves is open more than it should be. On the other
hand, they have an adverse eﬀect on the interaction if one or more of the radiator valves is
closed.[Ott19a]
In the article ”Test and evaluation of a method to identify heating system malfunctions by
using information from electronic heat cost allocators” by Dorte Skaarup Østergaard, Otto
Paulsen, Ida Bach Sørensen, Svend Svendsen, a test on the possibilities of monitoring the
performance of radiators and, if existing, any malfunctions, by the recorded data from the
heat cost allocators, was performed. The article tries to calculate the return temperature
and the heat load of a radiator based on the heat cost allocator. The method is associated
with a great degree of assumptions which are in many of the cases caused by inaccuracy in
the link between the theory and physical features of a radiator and its ambient environment.
The theory was tested as a case study on an apartment building from 1905 in Denmark with
eight apartments. Eight radiators were investigated. One of the ﬁndings was that in the
cases where a high-ﬂow through the riser, the neighbouring riser tend to have a ﬂow-back
resulting in measured higher return temperatures in the system than actually produced
by the radiators. The pattern of the control was monitored quite well so an increase or
a decrease in the return temperature was captured in many of the cases. However, the
Page 6 of 128 pages
==PAGE:21==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 2. State of the art
method seemed not consistent in capturing of the evolution and in some cases it completely
overshoot the magnitude of the return value by up to 10◦C. They also found that the
method could be used to ﬁnd any problematic radiators.
However, due to the nature of how heat cost allocator records data, sum recorded units
over an hour, a period with signiﬁcant ﬂuctuating control (on/oﬀpattern) are described
poorly by the method, which could be the origin of the failing of consistency in capturing
the evolution of the return temperature. Also, due to the method being heavily based on
assumptions of the radiators features and their environment, the method was quite sensitive,
and it was, at this stage, diﬃcult to detect when the estimation was directly transferable to
the real world return temperature. A sensitivity analysis of the estimated heat loads impact
on the calculated return temperature was also made. It showed that the calculated return
temperature was highly sensitive to the estimated return temperature, especially when the
temperature was high. A suggested approach to overcome many of the misleadings and the
unknowns were to adjust the method to a speciﬁc case, and the method of estimating the
radiator return temperature based on the heat cost allocators were not dismissed. They
concluded that the method needed more development in order to understand what it was
able to detect/monitor. [Ott19b]
The method and the ﬁndings of this article have a big inspiration in the approach for this
thesis and are founding a basis of a large portion of the data presented in this thesis.
2.1
Novelty
The novelty of this project is to develop a tool or service package which a building technician
can utilise to lower the supply and return temperature. This service package will be based
on measured data. The measured data will be translated to other parameters, such as a
calculated return temperature, and will be weighed against a calculated best-case scenario.
Page 7 of 128 pages
==PAGE:22==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 3. Methodology
3 Methodology
This thesis tries acquire knowledge of the possibilities of using existing sensors and controls
of existing buildings to evaluate a building’s performance. The way this is obtained are by
applying already known theory to explain the measurements of sensors. The approach of
the paper can be structured with 5 main points.
The ﬁrst point was to acquire the data and standardise it into a usable format. This was
obtained by the use of diﬀerent databases and the coding program called ”Python”.
Next step that is reported is an estimation of any abilities to lower the supply and return
temperature and to what extent, which could be used as a baseline of the building per-
formance. The heat degree day as well as the recorded yearly energy consumption of the
building are used to create a simple model to determine the potential lowest supply and
return temperature of the building.
The third objective the paper examined is the translation of the sensor data into usable
indicators. The method used to determine what data can be utilised to evaluate the per-
formance is trial and error.The calculated potential lowest return and supply temperature
are used as a baseline for some of the indicators as an comparison.
The fourth point addressed the creation of a tool. The tool is created based on the previous
ﬁndings of the paper. When the tool is developed, it is utilised to analyse a building case
to determine the heating system’s performance. The method for the development of the
tool is highly based on the option to replicate the analysis on other building cases. This is
obtained by converting the indicators to an interactive application.
The ﬁfth and last point addresses the use of the application. An evaluation is made of the
practice usability. The evaluation is based on feedback and the utilisation of the application.
A ﬁnal discussion is produced leading up to a conclusion on the estimated value of the
ﬁndings.
Page 8 of 128 pages
==PAGE:23==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 3. Methodology
3.1
Tools
The programs used to process this paper are listed below. All the modules used can be
seen in the appendix on the respective page with the code in which they are used.
• Anaconda
• Excel
• Jupyter
• VScode
• Python
• CSS
3.2
Order of reading
The thesis is structured in the natural order of the development of the service package.
First, is the reader presented for the case building and how the data gathering is made.
Then a model for a potential best case operation of the case building is made.
After this is a chapter regarding any potential performance indicators presented. A presen-
tation of the setup of the service package and the package in use in scenarios is presented
next. A ﬁnal discussion is produced, and the conclusion is written lastly.
A great deal of eﬀort has been put into making the service package generic, so it is rela-
tively easy to utilize the same approach in the next case and thereby develop the method.
Therefore, some code snippets are presented in the text if they have shown a crucial part
in the development and advancement of the service package.

S144622 - Kasper K.H.

Chapter 4. A case study
4 A case study
This thesis is based on a single building case placed in Viborg, Denmark. The building is a
multi-family apartment building from 1970. There are 42 apartments with 235 radiators on
three ﬂoors. The ﬂoor plan of the basement with the heating installations are represented
on ﬁgure 4.1. The placements of the risers are speciﬁed with red dots. The connection to
the district heating and the technical room is placed down in the left corner.
Figure 4.1: Illustrates the ﬂoor plan of the building case basement with the heating system
indicated.
The risers are between 2 to 3 storeys. Each apartment is equipped with a manifold. All
the appartment’s radiators are connected to this manifold in parallel through ﬂexible PEX
pipes that are built into the ﬂoor construction. The manifold on each ﬂoor is connected to
the main two-string distribution pipeline in the basement through vertical pipes–risers. An
isometric diagram over the branching of the piping system is made available. The diagram
can be seen at ﬁgure 4.2.

the results later in the paper.
The heating system for the building is divided at the supply from the primary side to the
secondary side by a heat exchanger. On the secondary side, the piping is divided into
two branches, see ﬁgure 4.2. The right side, the one going from 0-11, is monitored in a
higher resolution than the left side. Because the two sides are independent, each side can
be analysed individually.
Because there is tracked more information on the right branch of the heating system, this
is the one that is investigated in this thesis.
The heating system of the building is equipped with a domestic hot water tank and a heat
exchanger that is controlled by a weather compensator. The radiators in the building are
of panel radiator types. Figure 4.3 and ﬁgure 4.4 can be used to give a visual impression
of what condition the building is.

deck with a wooden ﬂoor on battens. The windows are low energy double panel windows.
The pump delivering the pressure for the building is a Grundfoss Magnum 80.

compensation settings and thereby the supply temperature for the building. The purpose
was to stimulate the residents to open closed radiators and thereby increase the heating
surface, which results in lower return temperatures. The weather compensation curves are

Chapter 5. Datacollection
5 Datacollection
5.1
Hardware
On the heating system, Brunata has mounted diﬀerent sensors. The two primary types
are temperature and energy meters. The sensors in the apartments are energy meters, also
called heat cost allocators of the type of ”future heat” from brunata’s product collection.
The sensors are adjusted to a placement of 1/3 or 1/4 of the radiator height. The sensor
has a metal-back plate that measures the heatﬂux from the radiators.
The design load of the radiator, as well as the placement of the sensor respectively to the
radiator, are coded into the sensor by a installer. Based on the placement and design load,
the sensor estimates the whole radiators heat output. These values are then sent to the
database. Because the values for each radiator are estimated upon this method, the actual
measurements have a natural inaccuracy, to what extend is not known.
The temperature meters are the ”Brunata Futura Comfort”. An external sensor has been
mounted on the piping which communicates with the ”futura comfort”. The sensor com-
municates the same way as ”future heat”.
5.2
Software/database access
The data for the project is gathered in two separate databases. The ﬁrst database is made
accessible by Viborg Varme. The data gathered here are the supply and return temperatures
and the energy used on the district heating part of the system. The energy is given with
domestic hot water usage, so to have a usable value, domestic hot water usage needs to be
monitored as well. The data is accessible via Viborg Varme web interface, where the data
can be downloaded as a .csv ﬁle. The interface of Viborg Varme is presented in ﬁgure 5.1.
Page 14 of 128 pages

the supply and return temperature for the whole building as well as the energy consump-
tion. The ﬁnest resolution available through the interface are daily accumulated energy
consumption and daily average temperatures.
The second database is Brunata’s. The database is called ”BrunataNet”. The database
stores the information for all the ”in house” sensors. The monitoring of the sensors are
stored for each of the sensors and can be grouped. The data can be pulled by the brunata’s
web interface as an .csv or .xlsx ﬁle. The sensors are grouped for each riser for pulling the
data in a more straightforward manner. A picture of the interface is presented at ﬁgure
5.2.

Chapter 6. Simple model
6 Potential
return
temperature
for
the case
A model is developed to have an accurate estimate of the best potential performance of
the building. Michele Tunzi, the Technical University of Denmark, created a model which
estimates the lowest possible temperature set for a building. The model is used as one of
the foundations upon which the building monitoring data is deliberated from.
A short description of the model is now made. The model ﬁrst estimates an eﬀective indoor
temperature on 17 ◦C. The model uses the assumed indoor temperature to calculate the
temperature diﬀerence from +12◦Cto -12◦Cto the indoor temperature. The sum of Heating
Degree Days (HDD) is calculated by looking at historical data for a year of the outdoor
temperature and ﬁnding the number of days with an average daily temperature between
+12 and -12◦C. Then the temperature diﬀerence from the indoor to the respective outdoor
temperature is multiplied by the number of days the respective outdoor temperature occur.
HDDtotal[To] = HDD[To] · nincidence[To] [◦C]
(6.1)
Where:
nincidence[To] is the amount of days on a year appearing at that temperature
To is the outdoor temperature
The energy consumption of the building case is recorded for previous years. The recorded
energy consumption is the total energy consumption of the building, including the Domestic
Hot Water (DHW). Therefore, it is assumed that 30% of the total energy consumption goes
to DHW.
Because the focus is on the radiators heat, losses by the pipes should be excluded. 30% of the
energy consumption of heating is estimated lost on heat losses. The building has an average
yearly energy consumption of 168[MWh] based on ﬁve years. The energy consumption is
assumed to be 343[MWh]·0.7 · 0.7 = 168[MWh].
Page 17 of 128 pages
==PAGE:33==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 6. Simple model
The energy consumption is used to determine the HDD, which is a heat demand coeﬃcient
for the building. This can be estimated in the equation below.
G =
Φ
HDDTotal · 24 = 2.4
kW
K

(6.2)
Where:
G is the heat demand coeﬃcient
Φ is the recorded energy consumption of the building case of a year
Because the HDD rely on the actual measured outdoor temperatures, which vary, one could
approach over several years to get an average in the building’s heat demand, which would
be more representative of the building and not only for the given that year.
When knowing the heat demand coeﬃcient, the expected heat demand on a day with an
arbitrary temperature can be estimated and by that also the expected heat load(P) of the
building.
Q[To] = HDD[To] · G [kW]
(6.3)
By the heat cost allocators, the heating capacity of each radiator in the building is known.
The heat cost allocators assume reference temperatures at (90/70◦C), so the heating capac-
ity is based on that temperature set. Summing the heating capacity of every radiator, the
design heat loads were summed to 296 [kW] at a temperature setting of (90/70◦C) (Q0).
The relative heat load can then be used to describe a required logarithmic mean temperature
diﬀerence (LMTD).
LMTD[To] =
Q[To]
Q0
 1
n
· LMTD0 [◦C]
(6.4)
Where:
n is the radiator exponent
LMTD is the LMTD at a design load temperature diﬀerence. A reference temperature of
(90/70◦C) is assumed in this case.
Page 18 of 128 pages
==PAGE:34==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 6. Simple model
Next is the required temperature diﬀerence found to obtain the heat demand of the building.
The actual design load for the building is at a temperature set of (70/40◦C). To estimate
the required temperatures for the building, the design heat load based on the heat cost
allocators needs to be converted to the building-speciﬁc temperature set.
The design heat load is converted to 137[kW] at the adjusted building temperature set
(70/40◦C). The buildings’ design heat load is used to calculate the design mass ﬂow of the
building.
mf = 137[kW] · ρ · cp · (70◦C −40◦C)
m3
s

(6.5)
Where:
Cp is the heat capacity of water
ρ is the density of water
The design mass ﬂow of the building mf was found to 1.09
h
m3
s
i
. The required temperature
diﬀerence can then be estimated by reversing 6.5 and isolate the temperature diﬀerence.
Instead of the whole buildings design heat load the expected heat load at diﬀerent outdoor
temperatures, equation 6.3, is used to determine the required temperature diﬀerence at
diﬀerent outdoor temperatures. It is assumed that the mass ﬂow of the heating system is
constant.
∆T[To] =
Q[To]
mf · cp · ρ
(6.6)
With the estimation of a required LMTD and ∆temperature, a goal seek function iterates
on the supply and return temperatures until matching return and supply temperatures are
found.
This way, a simple model based on HDD, design load, and yearly energy consumption can
estimate the potential lowest supply and return temperature to a building can be.

Chapter 6. Simple model
6.1
Setup
To to be useful in further work is the simple heat degree day model translated from excel
into python. The model should be made so a user of the program will be able to use the
model on any building by feeding as little information as possible to the program. The HDD
calculations are not building speciﬁc but location speciﬁc. Since the HDD calculations can
be used on other buildings and the analysis may be made on nearby buildings, the heating
degree days are created as a weather ﬁle that is loaded into python as an excel ﬁle, see
ﬁgure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Load the HDD ﬁle.
This ﬁle can be updated easily by loading in another excel ﬁle if the user wants to look at
another year.
The model then only needs ﬁve parameters to be able to run. The ﬁve parameters are listed
below.
• yearly consumption (MWh)
• heating capacity(90/70◦C) for the whole buildings heating system (kW)
• design temperature set for the heating system, this case (70/40◦C) (◦C)
• design indoor temperature in the apartments, 20◦C, used in LMTD equations
Based on these four parameters and the ﬁle containing the HDD to an outdoor temperature,
the rest of the parameters can be estimated. See ﬁgure 6.1. The model then has all the
information to do the analysis.
Page 20 of 128 pages
==PAGE:36==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 6. Simple model

are operation analysis with the goal of minimising, a function which minimise these two

Listing 6.1: Code for the minising function
As can be seen by the code, the analysis consists of four constraints. The ﬁrst two secures
that the supply temperature cannot be lower than the return temperature and that the
Page 21 of 128 pages
==PAGE:37==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 6. Simple model
return temperature cannot be lower than the ambient temperature. The next two tells
the model that the results should fulﬁl the required LMTD and the δtemperature, fond by
the HDD. The objective function is simply that ∆Trequired -(Tsupply −Treturn)=0. This will
then ﬁnd a temperature set that fulﬁls the required temperature diﬀerence as well as the
required LMTD, which is represented by a constrain.
The temperatures’ bound was determined to be in-between 10 and 100◦C. The function
needs to have some initial values to start the iterations, and these values are more or less
random.
The full code can be seen in appendix 02 and 03 for more information.
6.2
Analysis on building case
The model is used on the building case. The building case has on average a yearly total
energy consumption of 342 MWh over a period of 5 years. Some assumptions are made
to estimate this value. It is assumed that only 70% of the total energy consumption is
for space heating, and the rest (30%) are for the DHW. The next assumption is that
the radiators itself deliver only 70% of the space heating. The rest is assumed to come
from losses in pipes in the radiator. The energy consumption used in the HDD model is
342MWh · 70% · 70% = 168MWh.
The heating capacity for the heating system is 296 kW at 90/70 ◦C. The model is then
calculated, and the result on the potential lowest supply/return temperature as well as the
expected energy use at the corresponding outdoor temperature are listed in table 6.1.
The results show that the supply and return temperatures can be lowered signiﬁcantly
compared to the present building scenario. The results show that the building can poten-
tially be lowered to an average return temperature of 27.9 ◦C with a supply temperature of
35.5 ◦C when the temperature is weighted with the expected energy usage. At the design
temperature, -12 ◦C outdoor, the model estimates the required energy to be delivered at a
Page 22 of 128 pages
==PAGE:38==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 6. Simple model
supply temperature of 48.2 ◦C and a return temperature of 32.9◦C. However, as shown by
simulation in the article by Ala, Jarek and Kai, this is a quite low supply temperature, the
temperature setting of 45/35◦C can deliver heat at an outdoor temperature of -26◦C to a
modern apartment building.
6.3
Indicators of performance
The calculated potential return and supply temperature are used as indicators of perfor-
mance for further investigation on the measurements by the heat cost allocators.
The
assumption of how much of the total energy consumption used for the DHW is adjusted
because the actual recorded data on the energy consumption is made available. The total
consumption and the DHW consumption data are drawn on ﬁgure 6.2.
Figure 6.2: The total energy consumption and the DHW consumption from 1’st of June
2020 to 2’nd of June 2021. with lost data from the 10’th of December to the 16’th of
January.
The recorded data has a blacked period where the sensors were not in operation. This period
was from the 10’th of December to the 16’th of January. This will, of course, make an error
in what the actual energy consumption is. However, it is assumed that the recording of
the remaining 328 days are representative enough for the resolution of which this analysis
is made.

Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
7 Estimation of values upon sensor
data
As mentioned in chapter 5, a data collection system is established by Brunata. The system
has been measuring consumption on the heating system in the period of primo 2021 till
today. The data can be hard to extract any knowledge from in the form they are measured,
R(units). Therefore it could make sense to investigate the possibility of translating the
measured value into another unit and analysing the potential information.
To be able to work with the data, the data should be modiﬁed, so it corresponds to the same
unit and scale. The data from Brunata are on a scale of units. As mentioned previously,
the heat cost allocators and the other sensors are not time ﬁxed, which means that they do
not record in a ﬁxed timestamp. So to compare two sensors, a modiﬁcation of the data is
needed.
In an article called ”How to Interpolate Time Series Data in Python Pandas” by Jessica
Walkenhorst, she explains how it is possible to standardise the timestamp of the sensor read-
ings and how to ﬁll missing data stamp with nearby measurement either by interpolating,
forward-ﬁlling or backwards-ﬁlling.[Wal]
Using Jessica’s method, it is possible to load the ﬁles into the python script as a .csv ﬁle.
In that operation, it is possible to set the timestamp, of each record, as the index. The
method makes it possible to perform operations on the timestamps. The timestamps are
normalised by changing the timestamps to hourly values. Any timestamp which does not
contain any records is then perceived as empty.
To ﬁll out the missing data, an operation that interpolates the recorded values is performed.
By reassembling the data set from an irregular time series into a regular time series, it is
possible to perform a linear interpolation fairly easily. The times of which there are no
recorded data are thus ﬁlled with the linear interpolation. script 7.1
Page 26 of 128 pages
==PAGE:42==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
1 # resample
the
dataframe
and creates
empty row for each time
stamp
2 # that is not filled
3 df=df.resample(’h’).mean ()
4
5 # interpolate
and fill in the empty
rows
6 df=df.interpolate(method=’linear ’)
Listing 7.1: A modifed part of the code for easier understanding of the poorly interpolation
method
In ﬁgure 7.1 can be seen a plot of the measured data points from one heat cost allocators.
The red dots represent the recorded values from the heat cost allocators, and the blue dots
represent the interpolated values.
Figure 7.1: Illustrates the recorded data from the heat cost allocator for radiator 8064708
(trace 1-red markers) and the interpolated/rearranged data points(blue markers)
As it can be seen, the evolution is captured quite well. Even though this method probably
is one of the more eﬃcient ways to ﬁll in the missing data, there will be a minor error
due to the ”guessing” of evolution between two known values. If one looks closer into the
interpolated data’s outcome, it seems like sudden changes are captured fairly poor.
As it is shown in ﬁgure 7.2, the rapid change is not captured due to the shift in time
step and that the interpolation was in too coarse a resolution to capture the change. This
results in some cases in an artiﬁcial peak load and in this case a right-shifted peak load,
see ﬁgure 7.3 and ﬁgure 7.5. Comparing the two plots it is possible to see the the peak is
more profound in ﬁgure 7.3 which is wrong. The right peak is displayed in ﬁgure 7.5.

By upscaling the irregular dataset to a resolution of one minute, then performing the linear
interpolation and then downscaling the dataset to a time series of the desired resolution
of one hour, the movement is captured in the correct period, as seen in ﬁgure 7.4. The
method can be used to ”forward ﬁll” and ”backward ﬁll” as well. It is possible to use linear
interpolation with success because the data frame is manipulated to be a regular time series.
Page 28 of 128 pages
==PAGE:44==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data

Because data from the sensors are translated from the analogue signal to a digital form in
the sensors and the data is the sum of around 1-3 hours, it is estimated that any errors
from the sensor itself are more or less smoothed out. It is not expected to be essential to
prepare the data any further before they can be utilised for any analysis.

Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
7.0.1
Correction of recorded data storing
The paper’s author has become aware of how the heat cost allocators are informing the
database and how the data are stored.
The heat cost allocator transmits a signal every two minutes to a local gateway. The gateway
accumulates the data. Every hour the gateway transmits data to Brunata’s website(Brunata
Net). If there is no diﬀerence greater or equal to 0.1 units, then the communication is not
stored. This changes the perception of how the data handling should be done. Instead of
making a linear interpolation between each recorded data point, a method called forward
ﬁlling should have been used. There is, therefore, a mistake in the way the data is loaded
into the created application.
This error is not noticeable when the period between the last change is short. But the error
increases in size with the increase in the idle period. This knowledge was speciﬁed too late
in the process to change it in the model and the results. It is evaluated to give a minor
change in the outcome.
The model and the results are all based on the interpolation method. In future work, it
would probably be more accurate to change the method to a ”forward ﬁll” and only use
the interpolation in periods when the heat cost allocator sends a signal.
When the energy consumption of the radiator is small, and the radiator is in operation the
heat cost allocator does not record any data. It is therefore suggested to increase the deci-
mals of the recorded data to overcome this problem. The gateway is of type ”BrunataNet
GateReceiver 3.1”.
Page 30 of 128 pages
==PAGE:46==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
7.1
Translation of the recorded data
In the pursuit of any relations or indicators in the recorded data, it is attempted to calculate
any radiator features based on the recorded data from the heat cost allocators. This method
was performed in a smaller scale in a paper[Ott19b] by Dorte Skaarup and co. as mentioned
in chapter 2.
The calculation of the radiators features is translated to python. In the article by Dorte
and co., one of the radiator features estimated by the heat cost allocator, is the return
temperature. By manipulating the known theory, they describe the return temperature of
a radiator. The equation are shown in (7.1).
Tr[t] =
(Tm −Ta)[t](1−n) −(hm/H) · (Ts[t] −Ta)(1−n)
(1 −hm/H))
(
1
(1−n))
+ Ta
(7.1)
where:
Ts[t] is the supply temperature to the radiator at a given time.
Ta is the ambient temperature.
hm/H is the relative thermal height.
It is assumed that all the radiators in the case
buildings are panel radiators. For a panel radiator, the hm/H is estimated to be 0.737
however, this is considered to vary by each individual radiator by a magnitude of up to
10%.
n is the radiator exponent and should be between 1.2 and 1.33. In this case, it is assumed
to be 1.3.
(Tm-Ta)[t] is the excess radiator temperature measured by the heat cost allocator at the
relative thermal height hm/H at a given time and is described by (7.2).
Page 31 of 128 pages
==PAGE:47==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
Tm −Ta = (Tmb −Tma) ·
R[t]
RB
( 1
n2 )
(7.2)
Where:
R is the measured unit by the heat cost allocators.
n2 is the eﬀective radiator exponent. ”This exponent is applied to make up for the fact
that the built-in sensor that measures the indoor air temperature is often aﬀected by its
close proximity to the heating sources”[Ott19b]. It is estimated to be 1.43.
Tmb is the radiator temperature measured by the heat cost allocator in basic conditions,
57◦C.
Tma is the air temperature measured by the heat cost allocator in the basic conditions,
20◦C.
Rb is the basic heat count rate of heat cost allocator, which can be described by (7.3).
Rb = Qn · k · ∆T (nmeter)
b
(7.3)
Where:
k is a heat allocator constant and are based on the Rb. The k constant is given to be
(
1
13.5) · (
60
999984) by Brunata.
∆Tb is the logarithmic mean temperature diﬀerence at basic temperatures and is given to
be 34.67 .
n,meter is a radiator exponent coded in heat cost allocator and is informed to be 1.33 in
Brunata’s heat cost allocators.
Qn is the nominal heating power of the given radiator. In this case the heat capacity at
the reference temperatures (90/70◦C) is used.
As it might have been made clear, the return temperature is estimated by many assump-
tions, which makes the estimation quite sensitive to the assumptions.
As there are no
sensors on the individual radiator’s return temperatures the performance of the estimation
cannot be compared with the actual return temperature and evaluated.
Page 32 of 128 pages
==PAGE:48==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
However, there are sensors on the riser level of the return temperature. One way to estimate
the parameter’s performance is to accumulate the return temperatures of all radiators on
a riser and compare the outcome to the recorded values by the sensors. This is described
later in this chapter and chapter 8.
As there can be some inaccuracy in the estimation by the heat cost allocators, boundaries
are implemented in the code. The return temperature can be surmised never to fall below
the ambient temperature. It is therefore assumed that the return temperature is equal to
the ambient temperature when there is no new data recorded.
At the same time the performance of the radiators is presumed never to exceed a ∆T
between the supply and return temperature of less than 5 ◦C. The code can be seen in
script 7.4.

Listing 7.4: Calculation of the return temperature based on (7.1), (7.2) and (7.3)
The supply temperature used for the calculation is the values measured in the building. This
means that there might be a small diﬀerence between the actual supply temperature by the
radiator and the temperature used due to the heat loss in the pipes from the main pipe in
Page 33 of 128 pages
==PAGE:49==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
the building to the pipe just before the radiator. However, it is assumed that the diﬀerence
is negligible and that it is unrealistic for every radiator to be equipped with temperature
sensors in a building in a real life, nor are the records on that resolution available.
There are several factors in the calculation of the return temperature. It is easy to think
that the outputs of the equation are too approximate to give any appreciation of the real
world scenario. Unfortunately, it is only possible to validate the results with measurements
on riser level. So here it is necessary to refer to Dorte’s results as well.
She found that radiators that have a frequent ﬂuctuating pattern are not captured well by
this method, nor are radiators with faults such as a clogged radiator ﬁn, or if the indoor
temperature of the apartment varies signiﬁcantly from the estimated of 21 ◦C,[Ott19b].
The measured return temperature of each riser is recorded. So if the return temperature is
summed to riser level, a validation of the calculation can be made on that level.
In order to estimate the return temperature on riser level, the mass ﬂow of the radiators
needs to be described. When the mass ﬂow of the radiator is known, a mass ﬂow weighted
average of the return temperatures of the radiators can be found, which then will represent
the return temperature of the apartments. A mass ﬂow weighted average of the apartments
will then be used to estimate the return temperatures of the risers.
The need for a multi-index understanding of the data is now required, as the radiators now
need to be connected to an apartment and a riser in order for this approach to succeed.
This methodology is described later in this chapter, but a description of the method to get
the mass ﬂow is ﬁrst presented.
It is known from the thermodynamics that the mass ﬂow, or mass over time, has a relation
with the temperature diﬀerence and the heat capacity, which is equal to the heat load, see
equation (7.4).
Q[t] = m[t] · cp · ∆T[t]
(7.4)
Page 34 of 128 pages
==PAGE:50==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
By isolating the mass an equation for the mass ﬂow is found, see equation (7.5).
mflow =
Qrad
cp · (Tsupply −Treturn)
m3
s

(7.5)
The only problem with this equation is that the heat load is unknown.
So an equation which describes the heat load of the radiator is found, see equation (7.6).
Qrad = Runits · 0.71 · 1000
[W]
(7.6)
The translation from units to heat load is therefore a multiplication of the recorded units
with a magnitude. The scalar of 0.71 is provided by an empiric analyse done by Brunata. It
can therefore be expected that the heat load parameter is rather representative of what the
heat cost allocators record, whereas the return temperature, as mentioned, will presumably
at times not capture the exact magnitude of the temperature.
All the parameters to calculate the mass ﬂow are then known. The supply temperature is,
as for calculation of the return temperature, described by the measured supply temperature
for the whole building.
7.1.1
Multi-indexing
A sort of information table needs to be established in order for the model to understand
the relations of the radiators with its corresponding apartment and riser.
One method to accomplish this are by making a table that works like a encyclopedia with all
the sensors. In the ”encyclopedia” relations between the diﬀerent sensors can be described
such as which apartments they belong to or which sensors they are in the same riser as.
On table 7.1 are listed the most important columns of information in the encyclopedia.

The column named performance is the design heat load of the radiator or the summed for
the risers.
When the measurements of a sensor are loaded, ex. a heat cost allocator, the name of the
column with the recorded data has the sensor’s ID in it. The ID is represented by the ﬁrst
8 digits in the naming. The code then extracts the ﬁrst 8 digits for the loaded sensor data
and uses them to look up which radiator it is in the ”encyclopedia”. That way the code
combines the information of the sensor with the measurements based on the sensors ID.
This approach gives the ability to rename the measurements just by changing the name
in the ”encyclopedia” but also fairly easily adding any features/information to the radia-
tor/apartment/riser in the program. The most valuable feature and the reason this is done
is to sort the measurement in a hierarchy order and thereby get accumulated information
on radiator/apartment/riser level of the building.
The method is described graphically in ﬁgure 7.6. The approach paves the way for an
objective orientated programming. If this approach described in this thesis is utilised in
another context, it would presumably be a superior method to create objective oriented

7.1.2
Additionally indicators
As additionally potential indicators a list of parameters is calculated. At ﬁrst the percentage
usage of the radiator/apartment/riser is calculated. This parameter tells how much the
radiator/apartment/riser consumes compared to the design load. The parameter makes the
diﬀerent radiators/apartments/risers heat load comparable as the parameter is expressed in
percentage of what must be assumed to be the highest possible heat load. The parameter
tells in other words how much the gas pedal is pressed. The parameter is calculated by
(7.10) for the radiators and (7.11) for the apartments and risers.

The parameter do more or less represent an indicator of the activity of the heat cost
allocator. If the heat cost allocator has sent a consumption that is transferable to more
than 10 watts usage the heat cost allocators are considered to measure an actual usage and
not an error or an inaccuracy.

The parameter compares the heat load to the heat capacity at the reference temperature[90/70◦C].
This means the heat load is compared to a heat capacity at a constant supply and return
temperature. When the supply temperature is lowered the parameter will therefore also
show a lower percentage usage even though the heat load stays the same.
It could possibly be useful to have a parameter that calculates the percentage usage to the
heat capacity at the measured supply and return temperature. This will not be done in
this paper but could be made in further development.
To ease the comparability of the number of active radiators and to have an even easier
overview of how many radiators in operation in the apartments/risers, the fraction of radi-
ators in operation of the sum of available radiators in the apartment/risers are calculated.
The code uses the information relation established in subsection 7.1.1 to ﬁnd the number
of available radiators in an apartment/riser. The equation can be seen at (7.12).
n(%)apartmentin operation[t] =
P nradiatorson[t]
P nradiatorsavailable
· 100%
(7.12)
Because a potential optimised performance was calculated in chapter 6, the ﬁndings can be
used as a baseline, as mentioned in the chapter. The ﬁrst parameter which is premeditated
is the performance of the return temperatures. This is done by comparing the radiator
recorded return temperature, both the calculated by the signal from the heat cost allocators,
equation (7.1) and (7.7), and the measured return temperature, if any.
The calculation is quite simple. It simply subtracts the recorded return temperature from
the potential return temperature at the recorded outdoor temperature, from DMI, at the
time t.
∆Tfrom potential lowest temperature[t] = Treturn[t] −Tplrt[t] [∆◦C]
(7.13)
Where:
Page 40 of 128 pages
==PAGE:56==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
Treturn[t] is the calculated return temperature based on the heat cost allocators.
Tplrt[t] is the potential lowest return temperature found by the HDD in chapter 6
Because the HDD model estimates integers and the outdoor temperature is recorded as
real numbers with decimals, an interpolation between the results from the HDD models is
made. When the measured outdoor temperature fx. is measured to 1.6◦C the model makes
a linear interpolation between the results from the HDD model at 1◦C and 2◦C.
The parameter is calculated for each radiator/apartment/riser in the building. This means
that it is assumed that a balanced system has the same return temperature everywhere in
the system. It also makes it possible quickly to see how far a radiator/apartment/riser is
from a balanced control.
If there are any sensors on the return temperature of the respective radiator/apartmen-
t/riser, the recordings are compared to the potential lowest return temperature from the
HDD model. The approach is the same as for the heat cost allocator calculated return
temperature. The parameter is called ”(m)∆Tfrom potential lowest temperature”.
The last parameter which is calculated is the energy consumption from the potential lowest
energy consumption found in the HDD model. The way this is done is pretty close to
the comparison made for the return temperature. However, the comparison is a bit more
complex as the HDD model estimates the energy consumption of the whole building. To
convert the estimated energy consumption of the whole building to the risers, apartments
and radiators, it is assumed that in a balanced operation, every element or radiator in the
heating system operates at the same partload.
So by comparing the element’s heat capacity with the building’s heat capacity, the element’s
share of the energy consumption is estimated. The recorded energy consumption for the
radiators/apartments/risers is calculated by (7.6) and (7.8).
Page 41 of 128 pages
==PAGE:57==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
∆Qfrom lowest potential consumption[t] = Qradiator[t] −QHDD[t] · Qnradiator
Qnbuilding
[∆kWh]
(7.14)
where:
Qradiator is the heat load calculated by the heat cost allocators, equation (7.6) on radiator
level and equation (7.8) for the apartment and riser level
QHDD[t] is the heat load found by the HDD model at the outdoor temperature, measured
by DMI, at time t.
Qnradiator is the heat capacity of the radiators at (90/70◦C) or the sum of heat capacity of
the radiators in the apartment or riser.
The parameters make it possible to evaluate the performance of a building element on
the energy consumption calculated from the heat cost allocators instead of the return
temperature.
All the indicators are used on the building case in section 9.
7.2
Weather compensation curve
Another way to determine any faults in the system could be to look at the settings of the
weather compensation curve and if the system actually follows the settings. Because the
data from Brunata is presented as time series, the timestamps can be compared with data
from other databases at the same time period.
One idea of analysing the performance of the weather compensation mechanism is to link
the supply temperature in the system at a secondary site, by Brunata, with the outdoor
temperature for the same period measured by DMI. By comparing these two values, a
plot with the same dimensions as the weather compensation curves. If the curves then
are plotted on the graph, it could show to be quite easy to determine which weather
compensation setting the building are set to.
Page 42 of 128 pages
==PAGE:58==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 7. Performance of measured data
Because the data are rearranged into hourly values, as mentioned in chapter 7, and the data
from DMI are available in hourly values, it would make sense to look at this resolution. If
it shows that a resolution of days might be easier to read, then it is possible to create a
plot in that resolution.
7.2.1
Setup
In 2020 weather data from DMI was made publicly accessible for free, making this operation
possible in the eyes of a private company or operator. In the resolution of hours, the data
was downloaded a day at the time. The data ﬁles are containing the lowest, highest and
the mean outdoor temperature for each hour in the location of Viborg.
The supply temperature measurements were provided by the sensor ”33432 /.../ OPV
FREM FRA VC”. The placement of the sensor can be seen on ﬁgure 7.7.
Figure 7.7: Illustrates the placement of the sensor 33432 which measures the supply heat
on the secondary side of the heating system.
The values from this sensor are, as mentioned, not arranged in a ﬁxed time frame. However,
all data are rearranged to have the same timestamp.
In order to get any knowledge on the performance, the weather compensation curves needs
to be known.
The schematics of the weather compensation curves are received by the
building operator on the case building. The weather compensation curves are shown in
ﬁgure 7.8.

error can cause in the translation of the outdoor temperature to the supply temperature.
It could also be a local temperature diﬀerence for the temperature sensor connected to the
heating system compared with DMI’s sensor, which the data is from. This can be caused
by sun heating the building envelope, causing a higher local temperature. If the sensor then
is placed close to the building envelope, it will then measure a higher temperature than
there is in reality.

Chapter 8. Service package development
8 Service package development
For a building operator to utilise the previously mentioned analysis daily, a so-called service
package is developed. A service package should be understood as a combination of tools
and workﬂows compiled into a user experience (UX).
For the service package to be an eﬃcient supplement, the ideology of an HMI to a SCADA
system is tried applied.
The package will run in the communication architecture with the system act as an energy
management system (EMS) with ideas from a SCADA such as warnings.
The service
package will also be referred to as the EMS. The service package is introduced as a web-
based extension to already existing infrastructures.
A web-based extension gives more
ﬂexibility for data acquiring.
The main thing to notify is that the service package is not directly connected to the existing
infrastructures. The idea is that the EMS have access to the current systems databases by
an API. The EMS has then the opportunity to add data from sources easily.
Because the sensors only update in ranges of hours, the speed at which the information is
accessible on the databases is not essential. Therefore, the data are stored locally to reduce
the time the data and the calculation are loaded. They do not need to communicate with
diﬀerent servers when the operator is utilising the EMS. This gives some freedom as the
system only needs to be connected to the servers once every hour.
In ﬁgure 8.1 a diagram over the communication path is presented.
Page 52 of 128 pages
==PAGE:69==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
Figure 8.1: illustrates the Communication architecture Between the sensors to the service
package.
The service package is set up as a dashboard with diﬀerent layers of information levels in
which the operator can examine the building. The primary feature should be an overview
of the building’s overall performance over the latest period of data. The view will then
consist of some overall KPI’s based on real-time data being available.
The next feature should be a view where it is possible to access and investigate the historical
data of the building. In the view of historical data tools, which help visualise and indicate
trends, patterns and anomalies of the data should be in mente, so two diﬀerent approaches
are used. The real time data are represented in a view of the push setup. In the push setup
the information is pushed to the user. If any anomalies occur, the data will make the user
aware.
Page 53 of 128 pages
==PAGE:70==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
The historical data is presented in a pull setup. The data are made available for the user
in a pre-selected environment. However, the user analyses the pattern using the tools and
view provided and ”pull” the information. This type of setup makes it possible for multiple
views based on what type of information/patterns the user tries to detect. [AS13]
The setup of the two types of visualisations are therefore separated in two setup sections,
namely section 8.1.2 and 8.1.3.
8.1
Setup of a multi-page application
Because these two views support each other quite well but are built on two diﬀerent ap-
proaches, the need for a multi-page application is present. A natural method to accomplish
this is to build the dashboard as an web-based application. The dashboard is therefore
build in a stack of Python, Json, css and html. A tool that eases the process is utilised.
The tool is called plotly dash creating an environment, where the communication between
the four languages is managed.
The application is built with a section for each task for the code. The structure of the app
collating of scripts and data are shown on ﬁgure 8.2.
Figure 8.2: shows the structure of the application
All the calculations are made in one script. This makes it easier to make any maintenance or
Page 54 of 128 pages
==PAGE:71==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
extensions to the calculations made on the local database’s data. By pickling the outcome
of the data to a ﬁle, the diﬀerent views/pages have quick access to the data. So instead
of the operator needing to wait on a time-consuming process of loading data, the data are
depicted which is a rapid process.
Pickling or pickle dump are a process of turning the data sets and functions into byte streams
which are quite fast for the computer to read. It is mentioned because the operation of
pickling make it possible to make the calculations once every hour and then only saving
them locally. This operation opens the possibility of large data sets on building information
to be stored locally and viewed with ease and on devices with low computational powers.
The data can then be updated just by downloading a new conﬁguration ﬁle when new data
and calculations are made, which is approximately every hour. The calculations are thereby
only made once every update, which reduce the loading time for the operator and thereby
increase the chance of the dashboard being used.
For the layout of the application, a navigation bar (navbar) with dropdown menus is used.
The navbar are shown on ﬁgure 8.3.
Figure 8.3: Present the navbar of the application
For an easy organization of the views, all indicators are divided in small boxes named
widgets. The widgets gather information in a structured grid. The colour chosen for the
dashboard is the complimentary colours of blue and white.
These colours are selected
as they associate the user with reliability, knowledge and seriousness according to colour
theories.[Gro]
In ﬁgure 8.4 is illustrated two concept widgets. When the mouse hovers over one of the
widgets, the border around becomes darker, and the widget itself enlarges. This enhances
Page 55 of 128 pages
==PAGE:72==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
the focus on what is going on.
Figure 8.4: Illustrate the components which the views are build of in the dashboard.
As previously mentioned, the EMS are structured based on two types of approaches, namely
The ”push” and ”pull” method. The setup of the two approaches are described in the
sections 8.2 and 8.3 starting with the push boards.
Page 56 of 128 pages
==PAGE:73==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
8.2
Visualising of real time data
The real-time view is, as mentioned, thought as a push view. This type of setup makes
sense as a daily performance overview over the building. The view is built by two pages,
one for a daily timestamp and one for a hourly timestamp. The views are build by four
widgets and represented in ﬁgure 8.5.
Figure 8.5: Present the dashboard page which are meant to be the overview of the buildings
performance. A identical view are made over hourly data points.
The process of reading the overview is drawn with a red arrow over the view. The idea
behind this is that the operator glimpse over the three ﬁrst widgets and then end at the
graph widget. In the ﬁrst three widgets, following the line, are pre-attentive processing
thought into the presentation.
This is done in the ﬁrst two widgets with colour indicators and on the third with a red line
which indicates the potential best. The operator can therefore by taking a quick glimpse
over the widget have an idea of how the building are running. The individual widgets will
now be presented and described.
Page 57 of 128 pages
==PAGE:74==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
Starting with the ﬁrst widget in the top left corner. The ﬁrst widget is presented in ﬁgure
8.6.
Figure 8.6: Illustrates the widget with the performance of the building. Red dotted boxes
are drawn on to illustrate the diﬀerent information groups.
The widget represented in ﬁgure 8.6 is the ﬁrst place the user is meant to look. The widget
is divided into three information groups indicated with a red dotted box on ﬁgure 8.6. The
ﬁrst information the user will be presented by is the potential best performance of the
building for the last three days. This makes the baseline for the other groups, meaning that
the others are relative to these values. The data shown here is based on the simple model
described in chapter 6, which calculate the potential best performance of the building based
on the outdoor temperature.
The data represented in group one are then the output of the simple model on the weather
data from DMI. The latest day measured is to the right. The date of the day is shown when
the mouse is hovering over the value. Here it is apparent that the consumption could have
been 826 kWh with a supply and return temperature of 35.5◦C and 27.9◦C for the last day.
The second group in the widget represent the limits that are set on the colour indicators.
Page 58 of 128 pages
==PAGE:75==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
The ﬁrst row is for the energy consumption of the building. When the diﬀerence between
the measured energy consumption of the building and the calculated potential lowest energy
consumption exceeds 5% the cell becomes yellow in widget group 3. If the diﬀerence exceeds
10%, the consumption is double the potential lowest energy consumption, and the cell turns
red.
The same counts for the temperature. When the temperature diﬀerence between the mea-
sured supply or return temperature and the calculated potential lowest temperature diﬀers
more than 8◦C the cell turn yellow, and if the diﬀerence is more than 16◦C the cell turn red.
If the supply or return temperature is the same as the ambient temperature the building is
seen as turned oﬀand the cell turns black. This is the antecedent process for utilising the
pre-attentive processing technique.
The third group in the widget represents the actual performance of the building. The ﬁrst
row presents the diﬀerence between the measured energy consumption and the potential
lowest consumption. These are the numbers written on the cells, which means that for the
latest measured day, the building consumed 391 kWh more than it could if it was balanced
and followed the supply temperature of the lowest possible. Ergo the consumption was 378
kWh + 826 kWh = 1204 kWh. The measured consumption was therefore over 5% but
under 10% of the potential lowest energy consumption. Therefore the cell is yellow.
The two next rows work the same way for the supply and the return temperature. It is
noticed that the supply and return temperature are respectively 10.6 ◦C and 7.0◦C from
the potential lowest. It seems like there is a change in the performance of the building by
looking at the colour and the value from the previous day, which were 6.9◦C and 4.8◦C.
This is the idea behind having the last three values.
By comparing the cell’s colours or values, the operator can quickly get an overview of any
changes in the building performance over the small period and if there are any incitement
to examine the building further. This could also be illustrated by an arrow that shows the
Page 59 of 128 pages
==PAGE:76==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
degree of deviation from the day before to the present day. All these groups are represented
on the dashboard for the hourly values as well. Continuing to the next widget. In ﬁgure
8.7, the second widget is represented on ﬁgure 8.7.
Figure 8.7: Illustrates the widget with the 15 worst performing radiators based on the last
day.
In the widget, the 15 worst performing radiators are listed. The cells are coloured in the
same way as widget 1 with the same limits. The widget determines which radiators are the
worst performing by sorting by the last day from the radiator which are furthest away from
obtaining the potential return temperature. In the last three days, the worst performing
radiator is the one in staircase 4, apartment 1 th, living room. The radiator is 12.9 ◦C from
the best performance.
If the value would have been -12.9◦C, negative, the radiator would have rated the same,
as a negative value indicates the radiator to under-perform which could be caused by the
thermostat’s settings. Apartment ”4 1th” is represented multiple times in the widget, which
indicates an incitement to examine the building further.
Page 60 of 128 pages
==PAGE:77==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.

Chapter 8. Service package development
Because the radiators are rated upon the last day, the two other days, represented in the
columns, can indicate if a resident has changed the thermostat’s settings as the radiator
can go from a green rating to a red. If the operator has made a suggestion to a resident of
changing the settings of some of the radiators or if the resident had changed the thermostat’s
settings by own will, the operator has the opportunity to detect the change.
The scale of the indicators, or the limits for the colour changes of the indicators, have
been selected based on the dataset. The magnitude of the indicators needs to be tried and
developed further. It would be adjacent to look more into which level the indicators should
be to get the pointers/hints of bad performance pushed to the building technician so he
can take action upon the information.
Next is the third widget represented in ﬁgure 8.8.
Figure 8.8: Illustrates the widget with the weather compensation setting.
The third widget represents the weather compensation settings. The widget is divided into
three groups. The ﬁrst one is the setting of the graph, where the user can set the amount
of days from the present day that should be drawn on the graph. As a standard, the three
last days are drawn. The setting is changed to 7 days in this view.
The second group is the graph. The diﬀerent possible weather compensation settings are
drawn here. The dots represent the measured supply temperature, by Brunata, over the
Page 61 of 128 pages
==PAGE:78==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
measured outdoor temperature, by DMI. The red line represents the potential lowest calcu-
lated supply temperature. On the hourly dashboard, the dots are coloured based on which
time of the days the record is. On ﬁgure 8.9 the colour grouping can be seen.
Figure 8.9: Illustrates the graph on widget 3 with colour grouping over the recorded data
for the weather compensation setting.
The third group is the prediction of the setting, which tells the same story as the graph but
with numbers. The group guess which setting the building is controlled after by calculating
which of the dots’ settings are most represented by. The guess is based on the dots drawn
so if the operator changes the amount of data dots drawn the guess will change there is
now more or less data represented. The guess is based on the root mean square between
the measured data to the graphs of the weather setting curves.
The idea of the widget is that the user glimpses over the graph to see what type of setting
the building is performing after, and if it matches the one set. If it is not the case, the
user can start troubleshooting. The view also suggests too lower the setting by telling the
operator how far from the optimal, the settings of the weather compensation settings are.
If the recorded data are between two weather setting curves, the operator can look at the
predicted setting and get the setting which the recorded data is best represented by.

presented on ﬁgure 8.10.

parameters simultaneously. This way, the user has an opportunity to detect any relations.

this case, it is set to ”24”, which then makes a moving average over 24 periods i.e. one day,

matrix only on the SMA’s, the user can check this box.

The ”graph view” view is especially useful if the user wants to compare diﬀerent parameters.
Because the information is divided into two diﬀerent graphs, it is suggested that the view
is used when a building technician has located a problematic apartment and wants to look
at the performance of all the radiators in that apartment (and similar scenarios).
If the operator wants to relate movements of two diﬀerent parameters, such as the part load
of an apartment with amount of radiators in use or if a calculated parameter is compared
with the measured value, then the ”two-axes graph” view is suggested. This is done in
section 9.1.

Heat map based view
The ﬁrst heat map and historical based view are the view called ”View indicators”. The
view can be seen at ﬁgure 8.18
Figure 8.18: Illustrates the heat map view called ”View indicators”.
Figure 8.19 presents the ﬁrst widget of the ”View indicators” view.
Figure 8.19: Present the ﬁrst wid-
get of the ”View indicator” dash-
board.
Figure 8.20: Show the second wid-
get of the ”View indicator” dash-
board.
Page 70 of 128 pages
==PAGE:87==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
The ﬁrst option the user is presented with is the ”resolution for time series” drop-down.
This drop-down list allows the user to view the data in either hourly, daily or weekly
timestamps.
The ”kWh from lowest potential consumption” parameter is summed over the time stamp,
which means that the values presented by the parameter are the aggregated consumption
over the time period and then how far from the expected consumption. The two parameters
’Amount radiators in use(n)’ and ’percent rads activated(%)’ takes the max value, which
means ex. that if an apartment has experienced 100% of the radiators in use at any given
time during a day but only utilise 80% most of the time the value will be represented as
100%.
This is quite useful in relation with other parameters to detect faulty control of the radiators.
For the rest of the parameters, the presented values are the hourly, daily or weekly averages,
ex. the average measured daily return temperature of the risers.
The next drop-down list on widget 1 is the ”Level of data”. In this drop down, the user
selects which level of building hierarchy that should be presented. Here the user can select
either radiators, apartments or risers. These values are presented on the y-axis.
In the next drop-down list, called ”category of data”, is the parameter selected. This selects
what is presented by the colours, which is the z-axis.
The last selector presented on widget 1 is the ”date picker”. Here the user can select which
time period to show, which is presented at the x-axis.
On ﬁgure 8.20, the second widget is presented. The ﬁrst drop down list is called colourscale.
This drop down allows selecting diﬀerent types of colourscales.
The two main colourscales are discrete and continuous. The continuous colourscales will
show each value with a unique colour. This can be handy if the exact values are important
and have the same information as a normal graph.
Page 71 of 128 pages
==PAGE:88==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
The discrete colourscale show the data in ranges. This colourscale has less information,
however it is easier to fx. detect an apartment that performance diﬀerent from the others.
An example can be seen in ﬁgure8.18.
The last view created is the heat map based view called ”colourmap self selector”. The
view can be seen at ﬁgure 8.21.
Figure 8.21: Illustrates the heat map view called ”colourmap self selector”.
The ”colour map self selector” diﬀers mainly from the ”indicator view” view by the pos-
sibility of only illustrate some of the radiators/apartments/risers. In this view, a scenario
where fx. all bedrooms is plotted to see if radiators are turned on or oﬀ. This can tell if it
is normal to have the bedroom radiator turned oﬀand if it is seasonal dependent.
Page 72 of 128 pages
==PAGE:89==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 8. Service package development
As it is made clear, there are a lot of features and opportunities in these views.
This
is because the views are based on the ”pull-information” ideology where technician has
the opportunity to pursue a lot of diﬀerent problems in the system. Unlike the ”push-
information” views, the operator needs to know some patterns or how faults are represented
in order to detect them in the data.
Examples are made in chapter 9 as analysis where the service package is utilised to detect
information of the performance of the case building and to give suggestions of how to
improve the performance.
Chapter 9 try to combine the parameters, estimated in chapter 6 and the indicators deﬁned
in chapter 7 on the recorded data for the building case. The service package is utilised to
investigate the data and presumably detect imbalance in operation of the radiators.
