Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
9 Scenario analysis
In this section, the service package is applied on diﬀerent scenarios. Evaluations of the
diﬀerent parameters are also presented here if possible.
As a reminder from chapter 4, there were some changes in the weather compensation
settings. The changes are presented at ﬁgure 9.1 again. Before any changes occurred, the
weather compensation was set to 1.8. The ﬁrst lowering was 25’th of February. The second
was d. 12’th of March.
Figure 9.1: Illustrates the recorded supply temperature for the building case. On the plot
changes in the weather compensation settings are marked.
9.1
Evaluation of the calculated return temperature
As the return temperatures of the risers are calculated, with the same approach as equation
(7.7), it can be compared to the measured return temperature. This comparison can act as
an evaluation of the usability of the calculated return temperature, based on the heat cost
allocators, as an indicator of the performance of the radiators/apartments/risers.
First, the measured and the calculated return temperatures are compared at a period with
no changes to the weather compensation settings on the riser level. On ﬁgure 9.2, 9.3, 9.4,
9.5, 9.6, 9.7, 9.8, 9.9, 9.10, 9.11 and 9.12 the measured and calculated return temperatures
of the diﬀerent risers are plotted. There was no sensor on stairway 7 th, riser 11.

ture for riser 0, stairway 2 tv. The correlation is 0.62 for the hourly timestamp and 0.8 for
the two SMA(24) graphs.
It is seen that the calculated return temperature is following the same pattern as the
measured one. However, the diﬀerence or the error of the estimated return temperature is
at some points as high as 10 ◦C. The estimated return temperature is not computing with
a constant oﬀset.

2 th. The evolution of the return temperature over time is somehow captured. However,
there are a lot of noise which makes it too inaccurate to be a stable conﬁrmation or indicator
of what the return temperature of the riser is.

Looking at the comparison between the estimated mass ﬂow weighted return temperature
with the measured return temperature for the last risers, it is clear that there is no stable
oﬀset or constant precision of the estimation.
A lot of the time, the estimated return
temperature captures the progression of the actual return temperature.
However, there is no constancy in the error, and the magnitude of advance is almost con-
sistently exaggerated. Even when the data is smoothed on a daily average, the estimation
fails to picture the actual situation. The error is not easily detected.
Page 80 of 128 pages
==PAGE:97==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
When comparing the results with the conclusions from Dorte’s article, some of the same
patterns seem present. The periods where the temperatures are ﬂuctuating on a short time
basis are most of the time exaggerated.
Dorte and co. also explains how the measurements sometimes are aﬀected by ﬂow back by
higher return temperatures in the nearby risers.[Ott19b] The measured return temperatures
of the risers are therefore all plotted in a single plot to see if there is a pattern that supported
an error like that.
The plot is presented in ﬁgure 9.13.
Figure 9.13: Illustrates the plot of recorded return temperature of all the risers in the
building except riser 11, stairway 7 th.
It is seen that the riser, which was estimated the most precise, stairway 4tv, is the riser
with the highest return temperature of them all. The stairway 3th is the riser connected
to the main pipe after the 4tv in the ﬂow direction. The temperature of stairway 3th is 6.7
◦C which is averagely lower than the riser in stairway 4tv for the period.
It is seen on ﬁgure 9.5, that the stairway 3th is signiﬁcant more imprecise than riser 4tv,
see ﬁgure 9.6. This could be a consequence of the higher return temperature from riser 4tv
creeping into riser 3th and is interfering with the temperature sensor.
Page 81 of 128 pages
==PAGE:98==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
The same phenomenon is seen in the relation between riser 7tv and 6th and in a minor
degree between 6th and 6tv, and 6tv and 5th. However, even though the same relation
between 6tv and 5th is present the estimated return temperature riser 5th is higher than
the measured, see ﬁgure 9.9.
Again, it is seen that the pattern is not consistent. The diﬀerence in the correlation between
the hourly data and the SMA(24) indicates that the method is insuﬃcient at capturing small
changes. This is probably because there is only one value every hour. The correlation is
quite good between the estimated and the measured return temperature when a SMA(24)
is made on the data. It indicates that the method could possibly be more accurate if the
recorded data were of a higher resolution.
A correlation matrix between the measured supply temperature in the main loop, in the
basement and all the return temperatures of the risers was created. The resolution is the
hourly average recorded return temperature. The correlation matrix is derived from the
data between d. 5’th of March and d. 12’th of March. The correlation matrix is presented
on ﬁgure 9.14.

Page 83 of 128 pages
==PAGE:100==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.

Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
The correlation matrix shows that the measured return temperatures are overall following
the supply temperature rather well as expected. Another interesting result is that the riser
”6 TH” is not correlated particularly well with any of the other risers. The neighbouring
riser ”5 TH” does not relate in recorded return temperature with a value of -0.02. The riser
also has a poor relation with a value of 0.23.
Looking at the graphs at ﬁgure 9.13, it seems like riser 6tv burst to high temperatures in
rapid ﬂuctuations, which could indicate a fault in the sensor. Still, assuming the sensor
records accurate by the magnitude of the recorded data, it could also indicate an imbalance
of the hydraulic system. When looking at the curves, it looks like the return temperature
is increased rapidly when the return temperature decreases for the two neighboring risers.
This could be a pattern of the riser 6tv receiving an insuﬃcient ﬂow. It could therefore be
interesting to look further into any indicators that might show the same outcome, which
will be done in chapter 8 where all indicators are compiled to an application, called the
service package.
It is therefore concluded that an estimate on the return temperature on riser level by a
calculation on the heat cost allocator, and on that basis of a calculated mass ﬂow weighted
return temperature do not have either the precision nor the accuracy of describing the
actual scenario on an hourly basis with the interval of measuring of once every hour.
The complexity of what the data represent are probably too great to identify any real
information from the noise. It is, therefore, necessary to use measurements of the return
temperature on riser level to determine the performance of the return temperature. However
it is not concluded that the heat cost allocators cannot estimate at radiator or apartment
level.
It could be adjacent to increase the interval of stored data in the database to every 10 min.
or lower, instead of every hour, to see if it made the estimation more precise and accurate.

9.2
Evaluation of the calculated heat load
Correction: The central measured energy sensor is not placed on the right side of the
main pipe branch, see ﬁgure4.2 as ﬁrst informed but measures the whole buildings (both the
left and right branch) energy consumption. The radiator measured energy consumption is
therefore only a part of the central measured energy consumption. The radiator measured
energy consumption is based on 30 apartments, but should have been on all 43 apartments.
The new information was found too late in the process for the author to change the sections
regarding this. The sections are 9.2 and 9.3. A brief presentation of the new data is made
in subsection 9.3.1.
A way to evaluate the calculation of the heat load, or energy usage, of the radiators, based
on the heat cost allocators, equation (7.6) could be used to sum the energy consumption
from the heat cost allocators and compare them with the energy usage measured by an
energy meter. In this building case, a energy meter is installed to measure the energy usage
of the building case as many buildings are. The recorded data from this sensor is called
”central measured energy consumption”.
The building has 192 radiators on the right side which means that the signal of 192 heat cost
allocators needs to be translated to energy usage at each hour or day, summed to an hourly
or daily value for the building and then compared with measured energy consumption.
Fortunately, that procedure is fairly easy achievable by the service package. In chapter 7,
an approach to calculate the energy consumption of the building based on the heat cost
allocators. The equation was explained by (7.6), (7.8) and (7.9).The data representing the
heat cost allocator records are called ”Radiator measured energy consumption”.
Utilising the service package to make the central measured energy consumption and the
radiator measured energy consumption at building level is plotted.Recalling that the central
measured energy consumption were 70% of the total energy usage, assuming only 70%
leaves through the radiators, subtracted by the measured DHW energy consumption. This
is mentioned in chapter 6.
Page 85 of 128 pages
==PAGE:102==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
The plot is presented at ﬁgure 9.15. The radiators in the basement are included in the
radiator measured energy consumption.
Figure 9.15: Illustrates the energy consumption of the building, radiator measured by the
heat cost allocators(left axis) and central measured(right axis).
The resolution is daily
consumption.
As seen on the ﬁgure, the pattern of the energy consumption is captured quite well by
the heat cost allocators.
The correlation on daily energy consumption is 0.99 for the
represented 32 days. The central measured daily average was 796.5[kWh] and 497.4[kWh]
for the radiator measured. The central measured is therefore 299.1[kWh], or 60%, higher
than the one converted from the heat cost allocators.
It seems like the method of converting the measured units of the heat cost allocators to
energy usage consistently underestimate the heat load of the radiators. It could also indicate
that the assumed heat loss of 30% to piping is to conservative though it cannot be the full
explanation as it seems unlikely a heat loss of that magnitude was present.
Plotting the energy consumption for an hourly resolution, the correlation decreased to 0.81
for the 32 days. The plot is to blurry to see the evolution, so a period is chosen where
the building performs under normal condition and the operator did not lower the weather
compensation setting. The period is from d. 5/3 to d. 11/3. The plot is presented at ﬁgure
9.16.

Again it seems like the evolution of the energy usage is captured by the heat cost allocators.
However, it is ﬂuctuating quite a lot on this resolution compared with the central measured
energy consumption. It could be due to the period for recordings of sensor data are hourly
and that there was a misunderstanding of how the sensors saved the data in the database.
Still, it is more likely because of the uncertainties involved with the sensor itself.

The heat cost allocators mostly moderately correlate with the actual energy consumption
and are even sometimes only has a low correlation.
The correlation is 0.53 in average on daily basis with the hourly resolution for all 32 days.
This indicates that using the measurements on hourly resolution may be too uncertain. If
the resolution was daily in this period, the correlation is 0.99. So the daily resolution is
clearly more comparable.
Based on the ﬁndings, the energy usage calculated by the heat cost allocators captures the
evolution of the energy usage rather well on daily resolution. However, the magnitude of
the estimation is quite oﬀ(<50%) and the hourly resolution ﬂuctuates substantially. It
could therefore be interesting to look into equation (7.6) and see if the scalars should be
adjusted. On the hourly resolution, the evolution is only captured moderately.
A reason could be due to an error in the loading of the data as mentioned in section 7.0.1.
The error was that the data storing was unknown until late in the process and unfortunately
misunderstood. The error was assumed to have small importance, however it cannot be
Page 88 of 128 pages
==PAGE:105==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
excluded that it has somewhat of a signiﬁcance at the hourly resolution. The loading should
therefore be changed to forward ﬁlling method and examined again.
It is concluded that the heat cost allocators can be used to estimate the evolution of the
energy consumption of the building on a daily resolution and probably also actual energy
consumption if equation (7.6) is adjusted.
9.3
Adjusting the central- and radiator measured en-
ergy consumption
Correction: The central measured energy sensor is not placed on the right side of the
main pipe branch, see ﬁgure4.2 as ﬁrst informed but measures the whole buildings (both the
left and right branch) energy consumption. The radiator measured energy consumption is
therefore only a part of the central measured energy consumption. The radiator measured
energy consumption is based on 30 apartments, but should have been on all 43 apartments.
The new information was found too late in the process for the author to change the sections
regarding this. The sections are 9.2 and 9.3. A brief presentation of the new data is made
in subsection 9.3.1.
In section 9.2, it was found that the central measured- and radiator measured energy
consumption was highly correlated on the daily average resolution. However, the magnitude
was oﬀ. Most likely is the conversion from recorded units to a heat load in equation (7.6)
underestimating the actual heat load.
As the central measured energy consumption is converted to the energy consumption of the
radiator by assuming 30% is lost on the way to the radiators, the actual magnitude of (7.6)
cannot be established as the actual scalar.
But assuming the heat loss from the energy entering the building to the radiator is 30%,
the oﬀset of equation (7.6) is found. The equation is, as mentioned.
Qrad = Runits · 0.71 · 1000
[W]
(9.1)
Page 89 of 128 pages
==PAGE:106==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
If the radiator measured energy consumption by the heat cost allocators should have the
same magnitude as the central measured energy consumption, the 0.71 should be changed.
By changing the 0.71 to 1.13, the radiator measured energy consumption corresponds to
the central energy consumption both in magnitude and evolution.
Qrad = Runits · 1.13 · 1000
[W]
(9.2)
The radiator measured energy consumption is plotted in ﬁgure 9.17 with the new scalar
and a range of ±5%. The central measured energy consumption is also plotted.
Figure 9.17: Illustrates the energy consumption of the building, radiator measured by the
heat cost allocators with a new magnitude ± 5% sensitivity and central measured. The
resolution is daily consumption.

9.4
Energy consumption of building case
In chapter 6, a model to determine the energy usage of the building case was established.
Therefore, the model is combined with the measured energy consumption of a period where
the supply temperature were lowered.
With a period that contains both typical operation and operation with lowered supply
temperature, it is possible to both investigate the precision of the HDD model and detect
if the lowering of supply temperature has any impact on the overall energy consumption.
The period of interest is d. 6’th of March to d. 15’th of March. The temperature was
lowered d. 12’th of March.
The outdoor temperature is plotted for the period to detect two days which are similar in
temperature variation. The plot is presented at ﬁgure 9.20.
Figure 9.20: Present the outdoor temperature recorded by DMI. The period of interest are
d. 6’th of March to d. 15’th of March.
Two periods have been detected. From noon 6’th March to noon 7’th March, a temperature
close to the same outdoor temperatures as noon 14’th March to noon 15’th March was
experienced. The average temperature at d. 6’th was 3.8 ◦C and 4.6 ◦C at the period at d.
14’th March. The correlation between these two days is 0.82.
The supply temperature is plotted, see ﬁgure 9.21.

The HDD model describes the energy consumption by (9.3).
Qexpected = Tout · −2.421 + 41.157
(9.3)
The HDD model describes the energy consumption with a coeﬃcient of determination of
59 %. The linear regression gives the equation : Qexpected=Tout · −1.629 + 38.989. with a
coeﬃcient of determination of 59%.The HDD model is therefore estimating the consumption
moderately. The diﬀerence is 0.79, or 32% for the slope and 2.2, or 5% for the intercept.
In the higher temperatures, the measured energy consumption is generally higher than
expected. The HDD model is not representing the best ﬁt in the presented month, but it
is quite close.
Because the data only spans over a month, it is hard to determine if this is always the case
for the building.The higher consumption could indicate that the energy consumption is not
completely linearly related to the outdoor temperature or caused by an imbalance in the
system leading to a higher consumption than expected.
Compared with the delay in the heat demand, the plot indicates that a weather compen-
sation curve with a slower reaction time could be used. So a weather compensation curve
Page 95 of 128 pages

that makes use of the accumulated heat in the building. The weather compensation could
look into the pace of the change in the outdoor temperature and react accordingly. If the
change is at a slow pace, the weather compensation curve can have a delay to lower the
ﬂuctuations.
Based on the ﬁndings of this section, it seems like the HDD model estimates well for
longer periods. The HDD model is close to the linear regression, however there is a small
diﬀerence. It could be interesting to examine a period of one year and see if the diﬀerence
is still present. If that is the case, the HDD model should probably be slightly adjusted.
Because of the variance in the recorded data, the HDD model is assumed to estimate the
expected energy consumption to a satisfying degree.

heat load. This indicates that the radiator in the living room of apartment ”4 th st.th”

operates at full load in an attempt to heat the apartment. After the supply temperature
is lowered, the radiator cannot deliver the same amount of energy as the temperature set
changes the radiator’s heat capacity. Focusing on the two comparable days, 22’th and 27’th
of February, it is clear that apartment ”4 th 2.th” and ”4 th 1.th” have almost the same
heat load. Apartment ”4 th st.th” on the other hand, do only deliver 56% of the heat as it
did before. Apartment ”4 th 2.th” had an increase of 7% and apartment ”4 th 1.th” had a
decrease of 7%. The riser had an average heat load of 1769 [W] d. 22’th of February and a
average heat load 1590 [W] d. 27’th of February. Assuming the heat energy consumption
of these two similar days should be the same, the stairway is
179 [W] short or 10%.
The explanation of why apartment ”4 th 1.th” experiences heat shortage could be that it
delivers heat to its neighbours during normal operation. When the supply temperature is
lowered, the apartment beneath, apartment ”4 th st.th”, fails to provide the heat load that
kept the stairway in heat energy equilibrium. Apartment ”4 th 1.th” delivers more energy
to its downstairs neighbour due to temperature diﬀerences, and therefore lack to provide
the suﬃcient energy required.
Based on subsection 9.2, the heat loads can easily be higher than the ones presented. So the
reader should focus on the ratio between the numbers. The part load of the usage compared
with the heat capacity at [90/70◦C] is plotted for the riser in ﬁgure 9.33. The plot gives a
relative heat load which makes it possible to compare the operation of the radiators.

9.7
Neighbour problem
This scenario attempts to detect if the neighbour problem occurs. The neighbour problem
is the scenario where an apartment has to turn of the heat load of its radiators due to
neighbours having turned down or completely turned oﬀthe radiators. This results in one
apartment carrying the majority of the heat load for multiple apartments. The scenario is
illustrated on ﬁgure 9.39.
Figure 9.39: Illustrates the neighbour problem
The heat map is used to detect any occurrences of the neighbour problem. ”The resolution
of time series” is chosen as the settings for the heat map. Next is the ”level of data” set to
”apartment”. The category of data that could be a good indicator could be the part load
of the apartment. This value is a summation of the heat load of all the radiators in the
respective apartment over the total design load of the respective radiators, as mentioned in
chapter 7. In the ”date picker”, the period of interest can be predetermined. However, the
loaded data set is not that big, and the resolution of daily timestamps can fairly easy be
distinguished from each other.
The setup of the heat map is visualised in ﬁgure 9.40. The ”colour scale” is then set to one
of the discrete colourscales. A colourscale that is easy to comprehend is the ”ice/ﬁre colour
30%” which is the default chosen. The ”zmin” is set to 0% and the ”zmax” er set to 25%.
The settings can be seen in ﬁgure 9.41.
Page 114 of 128 pages
==PAGE:131==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
Figure 9.40:
Visualise the setup of the
heatmap.
Figure 9.41:
Visualise the view changer,
which determine the colourscale and the
colour scale min/max (in this case 0-25%).
A heat map is then created over the selected parameters. The heat map is shown at ﬁgure
9.42.
Figure 9.42: Illustrates the part load of each apartment over daily timestamps. An arrow
is added to show where on the heat map the focus is. The mean relative heat load for all
the apartments was around 10% for the period between d.4/3 to d.10/3.
Page 115 of 128 pages
==PAGE:132==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 9. Scenario analysis
As mention previously in the chapter, the discrete colour scales all have ”out of bounds”
colours of grey when the value are under zmin and dark red when the value are over zmax.
In this case, it means that the cell will turn dark red when the radiators in an apartment at
a day have a part load over 25% combined. Opposite, the cell will be grey if the combined
part load of the radiators in an apartment is under 0%.
When looking at the heat map on ﬁgure 9.42, there are no dark red values which mean
that there are no periods where any of the apartments experience a daily mean part load
over 25%. However, it is quickly seen that apartment ”4 th st th” has a period of close to
a week where the part load is 0%. At the same time, the other apartments in the stairway
have a quite high part load with the apartment above (”4 th 1 th”) having a high part load
compared to the rest of the building.
By hovering the mouse over the period, it is possible to see that apartment ”4 th 1 th” has
a part load of around 20% for the whole period of d. 2. March to d. 10’th of March. In
this period, apartment ”4 th st th” has a part load of 0% and on d. 7’th of March, the
part load rises to 0.3%. This could thus indicate a neighbour problem. When a case like
this is detected, the ﬁrst thing to do is to see if it is caused by few radiators heating the
whole apartment and therefore are running at an unnatural high part load. In this case, the
probability of this being the case are very low; however, it is a good practice to eliminate
as many unknowns as possible.
On ﬁgure 9.43, the percentage of radiator which have been active in the respectively period
is plotted. This value is as mention in chapter 7 the amount of radiators that have been
activated in the period over the total amount of radiators in the apartment,

is in that apartment. It delivers a heat part load close to the average part load but do
only use 50% of its radiators. This combination can be expected to give a higher return
temperature but this analysis is for another scenario.

9.8
Feedback on the service package
Some of the results generated by the service package were presented for a building tech-
nician from Viborg Bolig, and a project manager from Brunata. Based on a powerpoint
presentation containing over 100 slides, they gave their opinion on the usage and their
impression of the potential usability in the results generated by the service package.
Both could see the opportunity of performance monitoring the service package could pro-
vide. However, the presentation seemed too uninspiring as they only got through 1/5 of
the slides. The technician stated that he was colourblind, which made it nearly impossible
for him to see any colour diﬀerences on the heat map.
A colour scale in grey shades needs to be implemented for the technician to get any infor-
mation from the heat maps. The part with the radiators/apartments/risers part load and
the indicators of if a radiator was operating were seen as a potentially useful tool.
For the daily view, explained in section 8.2, it was commented that instead of having the
values given in how far from the potential best case, rather show the absolute values. If the
user wants to measure it against the best-case scenario, the user can do so by comparing
the numbers.
The feedback was fairly short, and not much time was used on the topic, resulting in the
two comments mentioned above.

Chapter 10. Final discussion
10 Final discussion
A software that builds on the sensors already present in a building has shown to be quite
descriptive of the performance of the building even though the data is sometimes imprecise.
There is a lot of potential in software like this, but the software needs to be adjusted and
tested with feedback.
Many of the ﬁndings in the report lead to the possibility of building technicians gaining
a beneﬁt from having an interactive information source on a building. A case where it is
presumed to have high value is in a scenario where one of the tenants is lacking heating
power.
The package has the potential to be a tool the building technician could use daily as a
supplement in his daily work. But the real test is to use it under the lowering of the supply
temperature. A test like that would prove which views that are usable. The possibilities
with an application that can handle data on this scale seems plenty. The process of creating
a software that could manage the data is performed, and it has shown the potential, but the
real task starts now. There is a lot of parameters and conclusions which can be investigated
in data at this size.
In the two scenarios where the supply temperature was lowered, the building technician
received complaints from apartments lacking heating power. In both cases, the building
technician had a hard time detecting any reasons other than the reduction in supply tem-
perature. This resulted in increasing the supply temperature again. Based on the data
treatment by the service package, it seems like the real reason for the lack of heat load
was that either the resident did not adjust his radiators and was utilising only utilising a
fraction of the available radiators or the neighbours not doing it, resulting in some of the
energy going to the neighbour.
Much more analyses were made, but they are not included in the thesis. The point of the
research is not to balance this speciﬁc building case but more to demonstrate some of the
possibilities that a software like the service package can provide. The analysis showed that
Page 123 of 128 pages
==PAGE:141==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 10. Final discussion
over 50% of the residences did not use the radiator in what is assumed to be the bedrooms.
23/30 apartments did have one bedroom where the radiators were not in use.
Under the analysis, it became clear that quite a few residents were reacting to the lowering
of the supply temperature. Only 5/30 of the apartments seems to have a change in the
radiator settings, or at least utilising more of the available radiators after the ﬁrst lowering
of the supply temperature. In the second lowering, there were only 4/30 apartments where
it is possible to detect a change in the radiator settings. Only 5/30 apartment utilised
less than 50% of their radiators. The building has experienced a lowering of the supply
temperature earlier than the period investigated in the thesis. Unfortunately, the period
was not documented/recorded. But it could be assumed that some residents were also
stimulated in that period. Some of the analysis is presented in appendix 04.
Working with such large data sets with diﬀerent origins has shown that naming is a crucial
process. Many misunderstandings have its root in diﬀerent names for the same thing. A
standardisation of how the sensors are named could be developed to prevent any double
naming.
Late in the process, it was informed how the heating cost allocators communicated with
and stored data in the database. The change in the loading of the data could have a impact
in some of the conclusions. The expected primary change would be in the amount, or size,
of the ﬂuctuating in the data translated from the heat cost allocator.
Based on new information from Brunata regarding equation (7.6) it became clear that it is
probably not accurate enough to convert the units to heat load by a constant. The constant
used is based on the operation temperatures and will change accordingly. The Constant
can be Incorporated in the software to be estimated. The estimation could be by comparing
the radiator and central measured energy and adjust the conversion accordingly. The high
correlation indicates that the conversion still can be made with a scalar. The scalar just
needs to be based on the operation temperatures.
Page 124 of 128 pages
==PAGE:142==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 11. Conclusion
11 Conclusion
Through developing a software and utilising it on existing historical data, the performance
of a building was attempted described. The data were from periods where a strategy to
lower the supply temperature was used. The strategy was investigated in order to evaluate
its eﬃciency.
The main aim of the thesis was to develop and investigate the potential of gathering large
amount of data and make it useful for an building operator that is implementing a low tem-
perature operation. It can be concluded that the aim was accomplished. It was not possible
to test the software in active use, but by analyses on historical data it showed possible to
extract valuable information of the performance. The software forms the foundation for
further development, focusing on the most valuable elements to active use.
Based on the outcome of the software, it can be concluded that the lowering of the supply
temperature did stimulate some residents to utilise more of the available radiators.
In the building case, only a minority of the residents was stimulated. The building is already
operating with low supply and return temperatures, possibly why only a minority adapts
in the investigated period. Some apartments contacted the building technician instead of
opening the thermostats in idle radiators. This indicates that the lowering of the supply
temperature does not stimulate all people. Further, it was detected that over 50% of the
apartments had, what is assumed to be, the bedroom turned oﬀ.
It can therefore not be expected to ever reach a optimal scenario where all controls after
the same parameters.
It should be taken into account that one or two rooms generally is unheated due partly
to unheated bedrooms. based on the outcome of the software a model considering this
assumption should give a more realistic picture of the reality.
The signals from the heat cost allocators show to have a high correlation with the central
measured energy meter. It can be concluded that the heat cost allocators can be used to
Page 125 of 128 pages
==PAGE:143==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 11. Conclusion
describe the evolution of the energy consumption. The heat cost allocator can be used to
detect idle radiators. The equation of converting recorded units to heat load underesti-
mate the heat consumption. The high relation between the radiator measured and central
measured energy consumption indicate that with an adjustment of the conversion, the heat
cost allocators will be able to estimate close to the actual heat loads of the radiators.
The heat cost allocators are, however, not suited for estimating the return temperatures of
the risers. The heat cost allocators are too imprecise and are ﬂuctuating too much to give
a usable estimation. Sensors on the return temperature should be mounted on the risers
instead.
It cannot be excluded that the heat cost allocators could estimate the return temperatures
with further development of the investigation of the data from the heat cost allocators. It
is not dismissed that the heat cost allocators can describe the return temperatures of the
radiators or the apartments.
It is possible to predict the settings of the weather compensation settings by the measured
supply temperature and the outdoor temperature.

Chapter 12. Further work
12 Further work
The service package has been shown to be able to explain and describe the state of perfor-
mance of a building case. However, the service package is used on only one building case.
If more time was available, the service package would be used on more cases.
Suppose other buildings show the same results. In that case, the service package should
be used by a building technician at the same time as lowering the supply temperature to
verify the software’s usability.
It would be interesting to use indoor temperature sensors in comparison with the estimations
in the service package. The indoor temperature sensors could be used to have an enhanced
estimation of the ambient environment for the heat cost allocators.
It could also be used to see the usage of the radiators in comparison with the indoor
temperature. That way, it is possible to identify whether or a low usage of the radiators
are because the residents want a lower indoor temperature or because they get the heat
from their neighbours.
It would also be interesting to examine the equation (7.6) in relation to the actual magnitude
of the energy usage and see if it is possible to adjust the scalars of the equation.
The code is written more or less procedural. To simplify the code, it would be an idea
to rewrite the core features as functions or objects. If there was more time to expand the
project, a cleanup of the code would have been made.
The parameter calculated in equation (7.11), that represent the relative usage, compares
to the temperature set that is programmed into the heat cost allocator[90/70◦C].
This means the heat load is compared to a heat capacity at a constant supply and return
temperature. It could possibly be useful to have a parameter that calculates the percentage
usage to the heat capacity at the measured supply and return temperature set.
Page 127 of 128 pages
==PAGE:145==
S144622 - Kasper K.H.
July 2021
Chapter 12. Further work
The parameter would have the possibility of showing, in an easier understandable, if a
radiator operates at the max capacity.
The plot of the energy consumption compared with the outdoor temperature indicated that
a lower slope of the weather compensation curve could be used. It would be interesting to
investigate weather compensation curves further.
There was detected a delay in the energy demand compared with the expected. It could
be interesting to look further into the period of the delays and see if they are constant.
A simpliﬁcation of the hydraulic model established in the article ”Strategy for low-temperature
operation of radiator systems using data from existing digital heat cost allocators” [Sve21].
The aim was to investigate if it was possible to describe the hydraulic system of a building
only by its main loop. The simpliﬁcation was made.
However, the comparison with the detailed model has not been made. In further work the
comparison between the simple and detailed model should be made. The code is included
in appendix 05.

Hold styr på ejendommens temperatur med 
Brunatas elektroniske temperaturmåler.
Egenskaber og funktioner 
Brunata Futura Comfort er en elektronisk temperatur-
måler. Temperaturmåleren foretager en temperatur-
måling hvert 2. minut og udregner på baggrund af hver 
måling gennemsnitsværdier, som vises i målerens dis-
play. 
 
Futura Comfort kan bruges til måling af rumtemperatur 
og som overvågning til at sikre, at rummet er korrekt 
opvarmet. Dette er vigtigt for et godt indeklima og en 
sund ejendom. 
 
Temperaturmåleren findes også med ekstern føler, 
hvilket betyder, at temperaturføleren kan opsættes 
et stykke fra selve måleren. Derfor kan måleren også 
anvendes til måling af temperatur på steder, man ikke 
umiddelbart kan komme til. Det kunne for eksempel 
være måling af temperatur i lukkede skakter eller på 
rør.  
Aflæsningsmuligheder
•	
Måleren har et letlæseligt display, hvor tempera-
turen kan aflæses. 
•	
Måleren har indbygget radiomodul og kan derfor 
indgå i BrunataNet, som er et radionetværk, der 
kan opsættes i alle typer ejendomme. Netværket 
opfanger data måleren og videresender dem til 
Brunata. Hvis ejendommen har Brunata Net, kan 
temperaturmålerne tilkobles WebMon, som er 
en del af Brunatas online portal. WebMon giver 
mulighed for at følge udviklingen i temperaturen.

==PAGE:153==
Ensure accurate allocation and correct billing 
of heating consumption with Brunata’s elec-
tronic heat cost allocator. 
Properties and functions
Brunata Futura Heat is an electronic meter for regis-
tering the heating consumption from a radiator. The 
meter makes it possible to register the individual 
heating consumption of each resident in a property. 
The meter has patented two-sensor measuring: one 
sensor measures the heat in the room and the other 
sensor measures the heat on the radiator. As a result, 
the meter measures all the heating which the radiator 
emits to the room (plus heat) and all the heat which 
the radiator absorbs from the room (minus heat), for 
instance heat from the sun or a wood-burning stove. 
Deducting the minus heat from the plus heat provides 
the heating actually consumed by the resident, who 
pays for exactly this heating.
The purpose of installing individual heat cost allocators 
is to ensure fair allocation of the property’s heating 
costs. The individual meters ensure that the residents 
only pay for their own consumption. Experience shows 
that individual measuring of heating consumption 
results in energy savings – which benefit both the envi-
ronment and the residents’ finances.
Reading options
•	
The meter has a reader-friendly display, where the 
resident can see this year’s and last year’s con-
sumption.
•	
The meter has a built-in radio module and can be 
read via Brunata DriveBy, which reads the meters 
at agreed times without Brunata needing to enter 
the flats. 
•	
The meter has a built-in radio module also allows 
reading via Brunata Net, which is a radio net-
work that can be installed in properties of every 
kind. The network collects data from the meters 
and transmits them to Brunata. If the property 
has Brunata Net, the heat cost allocators can be 
monitored via WebMon, which is part of Brunata’s 
online portal. WebMon allows both residents and 
administrator to monitor consumption develop-
ment and consumption patterns.  
Errors and amendments excepted

Facts
•	 The meter measures the heating consumption via internal or external sensor
•	 The meter measures temperatures within the range 0°C to 105°C, with external sensor right up to 125°C
•	 The display is updated every other minute
•	 The meter has a replaceable battery with a life time of up to 10 or 15 years
•	 The meter is supplied with a radio module, which transmits data to Brunata every other minute 24/7/365,   
       if Brunata Net is installed

and transmits data 24/7/365

• 	 Ensures correct billing for individual heating consumption
• 	 Can contribute to financial and environmental savings

Flexible and safe installation
Brunata Futura Heat is available in several versions 
with external radiator and/or room sensors. As a result, 
the meter can also be used where the best option is to 
place the sensors some distance from the meter itself, 
for instance if the radiator is built into the floor.
During installation, each meter is adjusted to the radia-
tor’s heating output using a scale. This ensures that 
the heating consumption is measured accurately and 
is comparable with the consumption in other locations 
where a Brunata Futura Heat is installed.

which is always on. The display alternately shows the

BrunataNet GateReceiver 3.1 (radio receiver)
The BrunataNet GateReceiver is used in Brunata­
Net metering systems, which incorporate different 
meters with transmitter modules. 
The positioning of the receiver must take into 
account conditions of transmission and reception. 
The number of receivers must also conform to 
the transmitting and receiving conditions within 
the individual installations. Typical locations are 
stairways or attics.
With GateReceiver 3.1 it is possible to receive 
both wireless M-Bus (wM-Bus) and IMR, which are 
Brunata’s protocol version 1 and 2 (Time Division 
Multiplexing). 
GateReceiver 3.1 receives various data protocols 
concurrently, and it can hold data from up to 8000 
different meters.

Normalize timeserie to hourly timestamp
This procedure will normalize the data to the same scale/timestamp
which makes it a bit easier to work with as well as understand
This section fills the coloumns by interpolating over the timestamps.
It takes the known values close to and then takes the mean between them
which is then placed at the timestamp. The approach is highly inspired
by: Jessica Walkenhorst
link: https://towardsdatascience.com/how-to-interpolate-time-series-data-in-apache-spark-
and-python-pandas-part-1-pandas-cff54d76a2ea

7/45
where:
A is the surface area of the radiator;
U is the heat transfer coefficient of the radiator;
Qn is the nominal heat output of the radiator at the test temperatures
Ts is the supply temperature to the radiator
Tr is the return temperature from the radiator.
Ta is the ambient temperature.
T_m is the mean radiator temperature
h_m is some height at the radiator.
h is the height at the buttom of the radiator h=0
hm/H heat cost allocator placement at the relative thermal height hm/H
k is estimated based on the basic heat count of theheat cost allocator RB
R Units of heat emitted
R_b is the counted units of heat in certain basic temperature conditions, ΔTB.
n is the radiator exponent, which normally takes values between 1.1 and 1.4 – most typically
around 1.3
n_meter Brunata allocators are coded with n,meter = 1.33
TmB is the radiator temperature measured by the heat cost allocator in the basic conditions
TaB is the air temperature measured by the heat cost allocator in the basic conditions
n2 is the effective radiator exponent
ΔTn is the logarithmic mean temperature difference at the test temperature.

# Radiator specific values
hm_H=0.737 #for a panel radiator
n = 1.3           
n2= 1.43         
TmB=57 #[C]
TaB=20 #[C]
TmB_TaB=TmB-TaB
#Heat cost allocater specific values
k=(1/13.5)*(60/999984)
ΔTB=34.67 
ΔTn=32.74
n_meter=1.33 
In [ ]:
tprint('defining the calculations on the radiators')
def ReturnTemperature(R_Units_of_heat_emitted ,Q_n,Ts):
    # Secure the return temperature does not get under 20 deC
    if np.isnan(R_Units_of_heat_emitted) or R_Units_of_heat_emitted==0 or np.isinf(R
        Tr=Ta
    else:
        R_b=Q_n*k*ΔTB**(n_meter)                                            #EQ 12
        Tm_Ta=TmB_TaB*(R_Units_of_heat_emitted /R_b)**(1/n2)                #EQ 13
        Tr =((Tm_Ta**(1-n)-(hm_H)*(Ts-Ta)**(1-n))/(1-hm_H))**(1/(1-n))+Ta   #EQ 9
        
        # Secure that the return temperature do not exceed the supply temperature
        # The temperature difference ΔT cannot be less than 5 DeC
        if Tr>Ts-5:
            Tr=Ts-5 
    return Tr


def RadiatorMassflow(Ts,Tr,Q_rad):
    # if delta temp is zero the value will be invalid as you can't devide with zero

8/45
Degree days Anaysis - simple method
A simple analysis of the building is made. The analysis is used
to estimate what the usage of the building should have been to
a given outdoor temperature. This estimation can be used to
describe the performance of the building/each radiator at a given
time. By making this
comparison the operator has an oppertunity
to varify the observation data.
The simple analysis can be deescribed as based primarily on the
heat demand coefficent of the building and the heating degree days.(HDD)
Load
First the HDD and the stepwise outdoor temperatures are loaded. These values are a estimation
only based on the
outdoor temperature and are therefore generic and can be used on all the buildings.
Input
There is some input values which are provided by the building design and performance.
these are included below.
    if Q_rad ==0:
        m_rad=0
    elif Ts-Tr == 0:
        m_rad =np.nan
    else:
        m_rad = Q_rad/((c_p_water*10**3)*(Ts-Tr))

        if np.isnan(m_rad) or m_rad==0 or np.isinf(m_rad):
            m_rad = 0
    return m_rad


def UsageRadiator(R_Units_of_heat_emitted):
    Q_rad=R_Units_of_heat_emitted *0.71*1000
    if np.isnan(Q_rad) or Q_rad==0 or np.isinf(Q_rad):
        Q_rad = 0
    return Q_rad

Ta_design=20 #C
n=1.3
LMTD_0=(Ts_design-Tr_design)/np.log((Ts_design-Ta_design)/(Tr_design-Ta_design))
LMTD70_40=(70-40)/np.log((70-Ta_design)/(40-Ta_design)) #LMTD FOR 70/40 temperature 

Design_heatload_70_40c=round(Design_heatload/((LMTD_0/LMTD70_40)**n),1) # kW design 
massflow_design=Design_heatload_70_40c/(4.2*30) # Design Mass flow rate of the SH sy

tprint('making a simple consumption estimation')
print('heatload at 70/40 temperatureset: ', Design_heatload_70_40c,'kW')
# Columns to calculate
var_name=['expect_Heatload[kW]','expect_Usage[kWh]','expect_Relative_heatload','LMTD
var_list=['heatload','Usage','relative_heatload','LMTD_req','ΔT_req','T_supply','T_r

for i in range(len(var_list)):
    globals()[var_list[i]] = []


for i in range(len(HDD)):
    # Calculations on the variables
    heatload.append(COP_build * HDD['HDD'][i])
    Usage.append(heatload[i] * HDD['Num of day '][i] * 24)
    relative_heatload.append(heatload[i]/Design_heatload)
    LMTD_req.append(relative_heatload[i]**(1/n)*LMTD_0)
    ΔT_req.append(heatload[i]/(4.2*massflow_design))
    
    # The Goal seek is perfomed on each of the outdoor temperatures
    obj_fcn = lambda x: ΔT_req[i] - (x[0] - x[1])                                   
    constraint = [{'type':'ineq', 'fun': lambda x: x[0] - x[1]},                    
                  {'type':'ineq', 'fun': lambda x: x[1]-21},                        
                  {'type':'eq'  , 'fun': lambda x: ((x[0]-x[1])/np.log((x[0]-Ta_desi
                  {'type':'eq'  , 'fun': lambda x:(ΔT_req[i] - (x[0] - x[1]))}]     

    x0 =[70,21]   # the initial values for the supply and return, just to get the go
    bounds = ((10,100),(10,100))  # set the bound of the supply and return temperatu

    result = minimize(obj_fcn, x0, method = 'SLSQP', bounds=bounds , constraints = c

    # check if the minimizing is succeeding if so fills in the supply and return tem
    if result.success== True:
        T_supply.append(result.x[0])
        T_return.append(result.x[1])
        
        # Energy weighted potential best return and supply temperatures
        Tr_ew.append(Usage[i]*T_return[i])
        Ts_ew.append(Usage[i]*T_supply[i])
    else:
        print(result.message)
    
# calculate the energy weighted supply and return temperatures
Tr_energy_weighted=sum(Tr_ew)/sum(Usage)
Ts_energy_weighted=sum(Ts_ew)/sum(Usage)
    
#Fills in the DF and clean the lists
for j in range(len(var_name)):
    HDD.insert(loc=len(HDD.columns),column=var_name[j],value=globals()[var_list[j]])
    del globals()[var_list[j]]

Riser level calculations
In this part the riser sumarized flow is calculated as well as the return temperature of the riser.
The return temperature are calculated based on the weighted mass flow of the sum of the mass
flows in the riser
First the recorded return temperatures of the risers are loaded in, if there are any.
then the data is loaded.

In [ ]:
# Mean squared error
def mse(true, pred): 
    return np.square(np.subtract(true,pred)).mean()

# Root mean squared error
def rmse(true, pred): 
    return np.sqrt(np.square(np.subtract(true,pred)).mean())

# def mse(true, pred): 
#     return np.sum((true - pred)**2)

# Mean absoloute error
def mae(true, pred):
    return np.sum(np.abs(true - pred))

tprint('starting hourly indicator - radiator calculation ')

# A df for the indicators
radiators_indicator_tr=pd.DataFrame()
radiators_indicator_tr_color=pd.DataFrame()

# globals()[radiator_namelist[0]]['ΔT_from_best_potential_Tr'].resample('d').mean().
for radiator in radiator_namelist:
    # Indicator days
    n=3
    # get the n last period for each radiator
    pd1 = pd.DataFrame(globals()[radiator]['ΔT_from_best_potential_Tr'].tail(n))
    # Rename the column to the radiators name
    pd1.rename(columns = {'ΔT_from_best_potential_Tr':radiator}, inplace=True)
    pd1=pd1.transpose()

    # Gather it in one df
    radiators_indicator_tr = pd.concat([radiators_indicator_tr,pd1],axis=0)
    
# sort for the worst on the last day by the absolute value
radiators_indicator_tr.sort_values(by=radiators_indicator_tr.columns[-1],ascending=F
# Make a copy from the indicator list
radiators_indicator_tr_color = radiators_indicator_tr.copy()

In [ ]:
# Make the color scale
for date in radiators_indicator_tr_color:
    for radiator in radiator_namelist:
        # divide the different radiator in the different indicators
        # If the return temperature are the same as T ambient then it means that the
        # not on so the indicator will be black
        if globals()[radiator]['T_return(C)'].loc[date]==Ta:
            radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date]=black_color
        # If the delta T is higher than the 
        elif abs(radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date])>=red_limit_temp:
            radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date]=red_limit_temp_color
        elif abs(radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date])>=yellow_limit_temp
            radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date]=yellow_limit_temp_color
        elif abs(radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date])<yellow_limit_temp:
            radiators_indicator_tr_color.loc[radiator,date]=green_color

# Free memory
del pd1
tprint('hourly indicator - radiator calculation Done')        
        
round(radiators_indicator_tr,2).to_numpy

# Creates a copy of the indicator df for the building to fill in with the colorcode
df_building_indicator_color=df_building_indicator.copy()
for column in df_supply_return.columns:
    for date in df_building_indicator.index:
        # Check if the temperature is equal to the ambient temperature
        if df_supply_return.loc[date,column]==Ta:
            #Checks if it is the return temperature
            if column==df_building_indicator.columns[0]:
                df_building_indicator_color.loc[date,column]=black_color
        #Checks if it is the supply or return temperature
        # If the delta T is higher than the 
        elif abs(df_building_indicator.loc[date,column])>=red_limit_temp:
            df_building_indicator_color.loc[date,column]=red_limit_temp_color
        elif abs(df_building_indicator.loc[date,column])>=yellow_limit_temp:
            df_building_indicator_color.loc[date,column]=yellow_limit_temp_color
        elif abs(df_building_indicator.loc[date,column])<yellow_limit_temp:
            df_building_indicator_color.loc[date,column]=green_color

Dashboard
This part defines the dashboard and starts it. This is mainly for analyzing the data and illustrate
it.
The dropdown list is made with a loop. This is mainly for the startup of the dashboard as
the dropdown list is updating with a
Dropdown categories are made.
In [ ]:
# Create a Pandas Excel writer using XlsxWriter as the engine.
if Output_to_excel==True:
    tprint('Printing results to outputfile')
    # Find the path
    PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
    DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../datasets").resolve()
    filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('Output_Radiator_readings.xlsx')
    # EXCEL writer
    writer = pd.ExcelWriter(filename, engine='xlsxwriter')

    # Write the readings from brunata to a sheet
    df.to_excel(writer, sheet_name='Brunatas')
    # Write the normalization of brunata to a sheet
    df_interpol.to_excel(writer, sheet_name='Normalization')
    # Write each dataframe to a different worksheet.
    for i in range(len(radiator_namelist)):
        globals()[radiator_namelist[i]].to_excel(writer, sheet_name=radiator_namelis

    # Close the Pandas Excel writer and output the Excel file.
    writer.save()
    tprint('Print done')
else:
    tprint('Print to excelfile not turned on')

In [ ]:
tprint('creating dropdown list values')
def dropdown_updater(list_of_variables,n_remove):
    global Drop_down_category
    global Tsupply_dropdownpick
    global R_accu_dropdownpick
    Tsupply_dropdownpick=-1
    R_accu_dropdownpick=-1
    Drop_down_category=[]

    for i in range(globals()[list_of_variables[0]].shape[1]):
        Drop_down_category.append({'label':globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i
        if globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='T_return(C)':
            Tsupply_dropdownpick=i
        elif globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='R_unit_accumulated':
            R_accu_dropdownpick=i
    Drop_down_category.append({'label':'Pause','value':''})

tprint(__name__)
if __name__ == '__main__':
    tprint('Cleaning variables from calculations script')
    
# Write all the variables of the calculation sheet to a file which can be loaded in 
    # Either this (py)
    PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
    DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
    filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
    # or this (notebook)
#     filename = r'~/OneDrive/DTU/Forår21/1. Speciale/Operatormodel/config/globalsav
    
    # Find all the variables in the sheet
    var_names=[item for item in dir() if not (item.startswith("__") or item.startswi
    var=[]
    #make list which can be pickled with all variables
    for variable in var_names:
        var.append(globals()[variable])
    var.append(var_names)
        
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno what it does but needs to be there
    with open(filename, 'wb') as f:
        dill.dump(var, f) # var=[item for item in dir() if not (item.startswith("__"
    f.close()

# %% Define a function to shut down the server when closing the window
def shutdown():
    func = request.environ.get('werkzeug.server.shutdown')
    if func is None:
        raise RuntimeError('Not running with the Werkzeug Server')
    func()

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False 
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable

        ## The prediction of the settings of weathercompensation
        weather_setting_daily=None
        error_weathercomp=None
        for i in range(len(supply_daily.columns)-2):
            i=i+1
            meassured=Tsupply_daily[-(period_selected):]
            predic=supply_daily[supply_daily.columns[i]].iloc[-(period_selected):]
            error_weathercomp=pd.concat([pd.DataFrame({supply_daily.columns[i]:
[mse(meassured,predic),
                                                                       
 rmse(meassured,predic),
                                                                       
 mae(meassured,predic)]},index=['mean square error',
                                                                                     
                   'root mean squared error',
                                                                                     
                   'Mean absolute error']),error_weathercomp],axis=1)
        # Takes the two best fits 
        weather_setting_daily=('Setting: ' + str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean 
squared error'].idxmin()))
        error_weathercomp=('The weather compensation setting is predicted to be: ' + 
str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared error'].idxmin())
                            +'  with the RMSE at: 
'+str(round(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared error'].min(),2))+
                            '\n the second best is setting: '+ 
                            str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].sort_values(ascending=True).index[1]) + 
                            '\n with a RMSE of: '+ 
                            str(round(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].sort_values(ascending=True)[1],2))),
        
        
        
        
        
        return fig_weather_compensation, error_weathercomp, weather_setting_daily

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False 
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable

9/9
        # Takes the two best fits 
        weather_setting=('Setting: ' + str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].idxmin()))
        error_weathercomp=('The weather compensation setting is predicted to be: ' + 
str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared error'].idxmin())
                            +'  with the RMSE at: 
'+str(round(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared error'].min(),2))+
                            '\n the second best is setting: '+ 
                            str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].sort_values(ascending=True).index[1]) + 
                            '\n with a RMSE of: '+ 
                            str(round(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].sort_values(ascending=True)[1],2))),
        
        
        
        
        
        return fig_weather_compensation, error_weathercomp, weather_setting

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False 
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable

        ## The prediction of the settings of weathercompensation
        weather_setting_daily=None
        error_weathercomp=None
        for i in range(len(supply_daily.columns)-2):
            i=i+1
            meassured=Tsupply_daily[-(period_selected):]
            predic=supply_daily[supply_daily.columns[i]].iloc[-(period_selected):]
            error_weathercomp=pd.concat([pd.DataFrame({supply_daily.columns[i]:
[mse(meassured,predic),
                                                                       
 rmse(meassured,predic),
                                                                       
 mae(meassured,predic)]},index=['mean square error',
                                                                                     
                   'root mean squared error',
                                                                                     
                   'Mean absolute error']),error_weathercomp],axis=1)
        # Takes the two best fits 
        weather_setting_daily=('Setting: ' + str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean 
squared error'].idxmin()))
        error_weathercomp=('The weather compensation setting is predicted to be: ' + 
str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared error'].idxmin())
                            +'  with the RMSE at: 
'+str(round(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared error'].min(),2))+
                            '\n the second best is setting: '+ 
                            str(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].sort_values(ascending=True).index[1]) + 
                            '\n with a RMSE of: '+ 
                            str(round(error_weathercomp.loc['root mean squared 
error'].sort_values(ascending=True)[1],2))),
        
        
        
        
        
        return fig_weather_compensation, error_weathercomp, weather_setting_daily

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False  
# if the file is accessible then the page will run as it should and this will load 
in the variables from the pickled file
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable
    def dropdown_updater(list_of_variables,n_remove):
        global Drop_down_category
        global Tsupply_dropdownpick
        global R_accu_dropdownpick
        Tsupply_dropdownpick=-1
        R_accu_dropdownpick=-1
        Drop_down_category=[]
        for i in range(globals()[list_of_variables[0]].shape[1]):
            Drop_down_category.append({'label':globals()
[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i],'value':i})
            if globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='T_return(C)':
                Tsupply_dropdownpick=i
            elif globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='R_unit_accumulated':
                R_accu_dropdownpick=i
        Drop_down_category.append({'label':'Pause','value':''})
    dropdown_updater(radiator_namelist,2)

import plotly
import plotly.express as px
import plotly.graph_objects as go 
import dash
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd                  # Excel manipulator or DF tool
#%% THE SETUP OF THE LAYOUT
#  ██████╗-██████╗-███████╗----███████╗███████╗████████╗██╗---██╗██████╗-
#  ██╔══██╗██╔══██╗██╔════╝----██╔════╝██╔════╝╚══██╔══╝██║---██║██╔══██╗
#  ██████╔╝██████╔╝█████╗------███████╗█████╗-----██║---██║---██║██████╔╝
#  ██╔═══╝-██╔══██╗██╔══╝------╚════██║██╔══╝-----██║---██║---██║██╔═══╝-
#  ██║-----██║--██║███████╗----███████║███████╗---██║---╚██████╔╝██║-----
#  ╚═╝-----╚═╝--╚═╝╚══════╝----╚══════╝╚══════╝---╚═╝----╚═════╝-╚═╝-----
fig = go.Figure()
fig2 = go.Figure()
fig.update_layout(
    margin=dict(l=50, r=1, t=1, b=1), height=300,)
fig2.update_layout(

    # Level selector
    def update_level(Checklist_of_level):
        Drop_down_options=[]
        Drop_down_options.append({'label':'Tsupply' , 'value': SupplyTempName})
        Drop_down_options.append({'label':'T_outdoor' , 'value': 'T_out'})
        for j in range(len(Checklist_of_level)):
            if Checklist_of_level[j]=='Rad':
                dropdown_updater(radiator_namelist,2)
                for i in range(len(radiator_namelist)):
                    Drop_down_options.append({'label': radiator_namelist[i], 
'value': radiator_namelist[i]})
            if Checklist_of_level[j]=='Apart':
                dropdown_updater(apartment_namelist,0)
                for i in range(len(apartment_namelist)):
                    Drop_down_options.append({'label': apartment_namelist[i], 
'value': apartment_namelist[i]})
            if Checklist_of_level[j]=='Riser':
                dropdown_updater(Riser_namelist,0)
                for i in range(len(Riser_namelist)):
                    Drop_down_options.append({'label': Riser_namelist[i], 'value': 
Riser_namelist[i]})
            
        return(Drop_down_options,Drop_down_category, Drop_down_category)

    def 
update_graph(my_dropdown,category_dropdown,Checklist_of_Graph_options,graph_mani_typ
e,start_date,end_date,graph_height_slider_1):
        ctx = dash.callback_context # calls the event that triggers a callback
        trigger_id = ctx.triggered[0]["prop_id"].split(".")[0] # 
        if my_dropdown is None or category_dropdown is None:
            raise PreventUpdate   # prevent the GUI to update if no data is put in
        else:
            fig = go.Figure()
            for i in range(len(my_dropdown)):
                # the predefined graphs
                ## Supply temperature
                if my_dropdown[i]==SupplyTempName:
                    if category_dropdown==Tsupply_dropdownpick:
                       
 fig.add_trace(go.Scatter(x=df_interpol.loc[start_date:end_date].index, 
y=df_interpol[my_dropdown[i]].loc[start_date:end_date],
                            mode=graph_mani_type,
                            name=SupplyTempName))
                    else:
                        continue
                ## Outdoor temperature   
                elif my_dropdown[i]=='T_out':
                    if category_dropdown==Tsupply_dropdownpick:
                       
 fig.add_trace(go.Scatter(x=T_out.loc[start_date:end_date].index, 
y=T_out['Middel'].loc[start_date:end_date], mode=graph_mani_type,name='Outdoor 
temperature'))
                        fig.add_trace(go.Scatter(name='Upper 
Bound',x=T_out.loc[start_date:end_date].index,y=T_out['Højeste'].loc[start_date:end_
date],mode='lines',marker=dict(color="#444"),line=dict(width=0),fillcolor='rgba(68, 
68, 68, 0.3)',fill='tonexty',showlegend=False))
                        fig.add_trace(go.Scatter(name='Lower 
Bound',x=T_out.loc[start_date:end_date].index,y=T_out['Laveste'].loc[start_date:end_
date],mode='lines',marker=dict(color="#444"),line=dict(width=0),fillcolor='rgba(68, 
68, 68, 0.3)',fill='tonexty',showlegend=False))
                    else:
                        continue
                # more generic created graphs
                else:
                    cat=[globals()[my_dropdown[i]].columns[category_dropdown]]
                    fig.add_trace(go.Scatter(x=globals()
[my_dropdown[i]].loc[start_date:end_date].index, y=globals()[my_dropdown[i]]
[cat[0]].loc[start_date:end_date],
                            mode=graph_mani_type,
                            line=dict(width= 1),
                            name=my_dropdown[i]))

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False  
# if the file is accessible then the page will run as it should and this will load 
in the variables from the pickled file
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable
    def dropdown_updater(list_of_variables,n_remove):
        global Drop_down_category
        global Tsupply_dropdownpick
        global R_accu_dropdownpick
        Tsupply_dropdownpick=-1
        R_accu_dropdownpick=-1
        Drop_down_category=[]
        for i in range(globals()[list_of_variables[0]].shape[1]):
            Drop_down_category.append({'label':globals()
[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i],'value':i})
            if globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='T_return(C)':
                Tsupply_dropdownpick=i
            elif globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='R_unit_accumulated':
                R_accu_dropdownpick=i
        Drop_down_category.append({'label':'Pause','value':''})
    dropdown_updater(radiator_namelist,2)

{str(str(cat[0])+'<br>'+str(choosen_dropdown[i])):globals()[choosen_dropdown[i]]

        else:

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False 
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable
    def dropdown_updater(list_of_variables,n_remove):
        global Drop_down_category
        global Tsupply_dropdownpick
        global R_accu_dropdownpick
        Tsupply_dropdownpick=-1
        R_accu_dropdownpick=-1
        Drop_down_category=[]
        for i in range(globals()[list_of_variables[0]].shape[1]):
            Drop_down_category.append({'label':globals()
[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i],'value':i})
            if globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='T_return(C)':
                Tsupply_dropdownpick=i
            elif globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='R_unit_accumulated':
                R_accu_dropdownpick=i
        Drop_down_category.append({'label':'Pause','value':''})
    dropdown_updater(radiator_namelist,2)

    ######### Level selector --------------------------------------------------
    @app.callback(Output(component_id='category_dropdown_heatmap', 
component_property='options'),
                Input(component_id='Checklist_of_heatmap', 
component_property='value'))
    # Level selector
    def update_level(Checklist_of_heatmap):
        for j in range(len(Checklist_of_heatmap)):
            if Checklist_of_heatmap[j]=='Rad':
                dropdown_updater(radiator_namelist,2)
            if Checklist_of_heatmap[j]=='Apart':
                dropdown_updater(apartment_namelist,0)
            if Checklist_of_heatmap[j]=='Riser':
                dropdown_updater(Riser_namelist,0)
        return(Drop_down_category)
    
     # Zmin zmax range to values
    @app.callback(
        [Output('input_zmin', 'value'),
        Output('input_zmax', 'value')],
        [Input('my_range_slider', 'value')])
    def update_input(my_range_slider):
        zmin=my_range_slider[0]
        zmax=my_range_slider[1]
        return (zmin,zmax)

        Input(component_id='date_picker', component_property='end_date')])

# %% Depickle the calculation variables
import dill
import pathlib # Used for finding the  path to the dataset
PATH=pathlib.Path(__file__).parent
DATA_PATH=PATH.joinpath("../config").resolve()
filename = DATA_PATH.joinpath('globalsave.pkl')
# See if path is available so the dash board can react on missing information
try:
    open(filename)
    file_available=True
except IOError:
    file_available=False 
if file_available is True:
    # LOAD THE PICKLED CALCULATION SCRIPT(DEPICKLE IT with dill)
    # LINK: https://stackoverflow.com/questions/48037433/loading-pickled-dill-file-
containing-dictionary-of-functions?
fbclid=IwAR1neYPvvOyNEcealrIwRTR6ABoA0al2bwm2dNreLMF36olAKlPHARST9VQ
    dill.settings['recurse'] = True # dunno if necessary but works with it soo....
    foo = dill.load(open(filename, "rb")) # Depickle the pkl file
    for i in range(len(foo[-1])):   # define to global values so yeah they are 
useable
        globals()[foo[-1][i]]=foo[i]  # match name with variable
    def dropdown_updater(list_of_variables,n_remove):
        global Drop_down_category
        global Tsupply_dropdownpick
        global R_accu_dropdownpick
        Tsupply_dropdownpick=-1
        R_accu_dropdownpick=-1
        Drop_down_category=[]
        for i in range(globals()[list_of_variables[0]].shape[1]):
            Drop_down_category.append({'label':globals()
[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i],'value':i})
            if globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='T_return(C)':
                Tsupply_dropdownpick=i
            elif globals()[list_of_variables[0]].columns[i]=='R_unit_accumulated':
                R_accu_dropdownpick=i
        Drop_down_category.append({'label':'Pause','value':''})
    dropdown_updater(radiator_namelist,2)

the expected energy consumption

order to redress the complaint
5. Try to guess which of the apartments which were the ones that 
complained

Simple hydraulic model - main loop riser
balance valve
This code will function as the dynamic model for Mejeri gården.
Load packages
First all the packages are loaded which are used for the analysis
Control parameters
Here is a list of the variables which are controlable or at least
some of them. The aim with this part is to make it easier for the
user to control the program and make sensitivity analysis or just
changes.
Because the aim is to make it easier all controlable parameter will
only be presented here if the author (I) finds it logically to do so.
Physical parameters
Inputs the physical parameters

# Temperatures
# T_s = 54.36 # supply temperature of the whole system [C]
# T_r = 33.18 # return temperature of the whole system [C]

# Loads
Q = 296377 # Watt

# system specific parameters
r_f = 0.2*10**(-3) # Roughness factor of the pipes
m_r_total = 75*(1.6667*10**(-5)) #152.51  # the total flow [m3/s]

# Water specific parameters
rho = 968    # density [kg/m^3]
cp = 4186    # heat capacity [J/kgK]
eta = 0.0005 # Dynamic viscosity [Pa*s]

2/8
Loading dataframe (DF)
This section will load the dataframe for all the sensors in the building.
First dataframe, which have alle historical data about all sensors, will be made.
Then later sub dataframes can be made by capturing the sensors
which are wanted for a specific analysis
Loads the data from either a xlsx or csv file
Adding columns to the DF
This section calculates the initial parameters
The parameters will be added to a column in
the DF.
First the part load is set. (could be you had a scenario)
Defines the columns that are wished calculated for the DF
Defines a function that fills out the columns with the corresponding equations for each row.
The massflow is calculated by the total return mass flow times the radiators partload of the
the total load. So the design powers of the radiator over the total design power of the building
times the total flow so you get the part flow.

def velocity(df, i): 
    # Velocity calculation
    if df.loc[i,'Mass flow (m3/s)']==0:
        print('velocity in: ', i,'is zero')
    else:
        df.loc[i,'Velocity (m/s)'] = df.loc[i,'Mass flow (m3/s)'] / (math.pi*(df.loc
        # reynolds number calculations
        df.loc[i,'Reynolds n.'] = rho*df.loc[i,'Di (mm)']*df.loc[i,'Velocity (m/s)']

        # Friction factor
        if df.loc[i,'Reynolds n.'] < 2000:
            df.loc[i,'Friction factor'] = 64/df.loc[i,'Reynolds n.']
        else:
            df.loc[i,'Friction factor'] = (0.25/((math.log10(((r_f/(df.loc[i,'Di (mm

        
        
def singleloss(df,row,index):
    #Check if there is a sum of single losses if there is one then it wont calculate
    # expect the user to have done so
    if not math.isnan(df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']):
        print('Single loss is already calculated row',row)
        pass
    else:    
        ''' single losses
         first step
        if the user has calculated the single losses him self then it wont calculate
        #Check if there is a dimension(diameter) if not then it won't look at the re
        if math.isnan(df.loc[row,'Di (mm)']):
            print('Missing cross-section row',row)
            pass
        else:
            print('Calculating single losses row',row)
            df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=0
            ### indsnævring
            if index==0:
                print('first pipe section in branch so no expansion/reduction in row
                pass
            #checks if the section before has a dimension and not a branch and not a
            elif not math.isnan(df.loc[row-1,'Di (mm)']) and math.isnan(df.loc[row,'
                print('no branch, runup and valve connected to row: ', row)
                # add to sum K_L if there is a reduction/expansion in the pipe
                d1_d2=df.loc[row-1,'Di (mm)']/df.loc[row,'Di (mm)']
                if d1_d2<0.25:
                    df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']+1
                elif d1_d2<0.4:
                    df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']+0.9
                elif d1_d2<0.6:
                    df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']+0.6
                elif d1_d2<0.8:
                    df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']+0.3
                elif d1_d2<1:
                    df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']+0.1
                    
        # angle bend losses
        if not math.isnan(df.loc[row,'angle bend']):
            if df.loc[row,'Di (mm)']<10:
                print('anglebend is <10 row: ',row)
                df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']=df.loc[row,'Sum K_L']+2.5*df.loc[row,'angle be
            elif df.loc[row,'Di (mm)']<17.5:

# checks if there is an unbalance in the last row
for i in range(len(df_print)):
    if df_print.loc[i,columns[len(columns)-1]]==0:
        df_print.loc[i,columns[len(columns)-1]]=' '

# Convert the dataframe to an XlsxWriter Excel object.
df_print.to_excel(writer, sheet_name='Output',index = False, header=True)

# Get the xlsxwriter objects from the dataframe writer object.
workbook  = writer.book
worksheet = writer.sheets['Output']

# setup formatting of the cells.
cell_format = workbook.add_format({'bold': True})
cell_format.set_align('center')
